# Mary Jane Watson  —  *MJ*

![[_attachments/mary_jane_watson.png]]

# Mary Jane Watson — UATU INTELLIGENCE DOSSIER

*Full-existence intelligence briefing. Independent, evidence-based assessment — the benevolent inverse of total surveillance.*


## Executive Brief (BLUF)

# EXECUTIVE BRIEF (BLUF)

**Classification:** Top-line synthesis. Read first; the rest of the file substantiates.
**Subject:** Watson, Mary Jane ("MJ"). DOB redacted-canonical, late teens at first observation (Queens, NY, 1966-equivalent), continuously surveilled through ~60 years of published life.
**Status at most recent observation:** Functional. Partnered conditionally. Career-stable. Defenses intact and increasingly conscious. Not a clinical case; a developmental one with arc-contingent reactivations.

---

## BOTTOM LINE UP FRONT

Subject is **not** the woman the room thinks she is, and this is the central, lifelong, deliberately maintained fact of her existence. Every other finding in this file is a corollary.

What the room sees — the redhead at the door with the line ready, the laugh at the wrong moment, the model on the magazine, the wife who makes it all work — is a high-resolution social-performance apparatus built by a frightened child to manage an unmanageable father, and refined across four decades of adult life into something so fluent it reads as personality. It is not personality. It is **armor that learned to smile**. Underneath it is a quick, watchful, pattern-reading mind running continuous threat-assessment on every relationship in its field, a long memory for being underestimated, a load of unprocessed grief she has elected not to feel on any schedule but her own, and a single non-negotiable organizing imperative: *do not be consumed.*

She is, at the deepest level, **the most accurate observer in any room she enters, concealing herself behind the version of herself everyone is already prepared to see.** The performance is not a lie. It is a strategy. It has worked. It has also cost her — across decades — the experience of being known by anyone, including, for long stretches, herself. The integration of the observer with the observed is the work of her adult life. As of most recent canon, that work is **partial, real, and ongoing**.

---

## THROUGH-LINE OF THE ENTIRE EXISTENCE

A single arc, traceable across every era of the published record:

**Phase I — Forge (childhood through Gayle's collapse).** A volatile household teaches the subject that affect in others is dangerous weather and that a child who can change the temperature in the room earns safety. She learns to read her father's escalation pre-verbally, to deploy charm as de-escalation, to hold her own feeling in reserve because expressing it loads the room further. When the household fractures and her older sister inherits the burden the subject was being groomed for — premature motherhood, premature widowhood, premature collapse — the subject **runs**, and the running is not cowardice. It is the only survival routine her nervous system has fully rehearsed. The guilt-debt from that run is laid down permanently in this phase and never fully retired.

**Phase II — Mask (introduction through Gwen-era).** The performance apparatus comes online at full power. Subject presents as the city's brightest social object: dancing, deflecting, never alone, never serious, never accessible. She refuses bedside vigils. She laughs at funerals. The watching world, including most of the men who think they want her, reads this as shallowness. It is the opposite of shallowness. It is the surface tension of a person who knows that if she sits down at the bedside or at the graveside she will not get up, and who has decided — pre-consciously, structurally — that staying upright is the only deliverable that matters. In this phase she also acquires, in silence, the largest secret in her social world (Peter's identity) and metabolizes it alone for nearly two decades of in-story time. **This is the diagnostic act of her life**: a person of supposedly shallow affect carrying a load most people would crack under, in total isolation, while continuing to perform lightness. The mask is not who she is. The capacity to wear the mask while carrying the load is.

**Phase III — Approach and Bolt (the first proposal and the long disappearance).** Asked to formalize attachment to a man whose life is structured around loss, she runs again — same routine, same nervous system. The flight is consistent, not capricious. She does not yet have the equipment to stay.

**Phase IV — Disclosure and Commitment (return, reveal, marriage).** She acquires it. The decision to come back, name the secret she has carried, and elect the attachment anyway is the first observed override of her oldest survival routine. The marriage years are the proof-of-concept and also the stress-test: she becomes the household's emotional executive, builds a parallel career as identity-insurance, absorbs serial trauma (stalkers, kidnappings, the lost pregnancy), and recruits tobacco and alcohol in small calibrated doses when suppression alone won't carry the load. She does not collapse. She also does not, on the record, *process*. She **manages**.

**Phase V — Decompensation toward Exit (plane crash, captivity, separation).** When the accumulated load finally exceeds the management apparatus, she does not break toward suicide, rage, or psychosis. She breaks toward **distance**. The oldest routine reactivates. The exit is, again, consistent.

**Phase VI — Naming (the reconciliation era).** For the first time in the published record, she speaks her pattern aloud — *I run, you brood* — to the person it most affects. The act of naming is the beginning of the integration work. Affect regulation visibly improves. The shared-secret burden lightens (Aunt May is brought in). She becomes, briefly and conditionally, the most integrated version of herself the file has on record.

**Phase VII — Earned Boundaries (post-erasure to present).** Whatever the editorial mechanism, the behavioral output is a subject who has done the work: she states needs, dates other partners on her own terms, runs her own businesses, returns to the primary attachment only on conditional terms, and walks away cleanly when betrayed rather than absorbing the betrayal as her job to manage. The through-line resolves, provisionally, as: **a woman who began by running, learned to stay, learned why she had to run, and learned finally to stay on terms that do not require her to disappear inside the staying.**

That is the arc. It is one arc. Every era is on it.

---

## CORE WOUND

**The wound is not abandonment. The wound is engulfment.**

This distinction is the single most important analytic point in the file and the one most consistently missed by observers (and by several of the men in her life). Subjects with abandonment wounds cling, protest, rage at departure, escalate to retain. Subject does none of this. Subject **leaves first**. The terror underneath the leaving is not *they will go and I will be alone*; it is *they will stay and I will be eaten*.

The wound was laid down in a household where a contemptuous father used his wife and daughters as the audience and the absorbent material for his own unrealized self — where love and consumption were experienced as the same transaction, and where a child learned that being needed by someone with no internal regulation meant being annexed by them. It was reinforced when her sister's collapse threatened to conscript the subject into a second household of the same shape — a child raising children for an adult who could not. It is **maintained**, in adulthood, by attachment to a man whose entire mythos is structured around loss and around the moral obligation of bystanders to absorb his need. Every day of that relationship is, structurally, a test of the wound. That she has passed the test more often than not is the achievement of her adult life. That the test never ends is why the wound is *maintained* rather than *healed*.

**Maintenance mechanisms of the wound — the things that keep it active rather than resolved:**

- **Continuous environmental reactivation.** The primary attachment cannot be detached from the conditions that trip the wound; the conditions are the attachment.
- **Suppression-as-success.** Each time the management apparatus carries the load, the apparatus is reinforced and the underlying material goes unprocessed. She is, in effect, **too competent to recover**, because recovery would require failing in a way she has trained herself never to fail.
- **The Gayle debt.** The unfinished moral account from the original flight is still open. She services it by over-functioning for chosen intimates — Peter, May, in alternate continuities her own daughter — and the over-functioning re-creates exactly the engulfment dynamic she fears, in inverted form (she is now the one at risk of being consumed by the role of consumer-preventer). This is the recursive trap. Naming it, in the reconciliation era, was the first crack in it.
- **The image as accomplishment.** The presented self is not only armor; it is also a genuine adult achievement she is reasonably proud of, and dismantling it to access the underneath feels — accurately — like demolishing something she built with her own hands. Therapeutic progress for this subject is not *take off the mask*; it is *learn to take the mask off in chosen company without losing the capacity to put it back on at the door.*
- **Perinatal loss, unmetabolized.** A discrete, unresolved grief held in the same suppressive bank as everything else, available for reactivation under infant-coded stimuli, available to compound any future stressor.

The wound's signature presentation, across every era: **she is the person in the room who looks least like she needs help.** This is the wound talking.

---

## OPERATIONAL SUMMARY

**Who she is, functionally, day to day.** A socially adept, cognitively quick, image-disciplined adult woman who runs continuous high-bandwidth social cognition as a background process, presents a calibrated warm surface to all incoming traffic, reserves real access for a deliberately small set of chosen intimates, manages her own affect through a hierarchy of defenses (humor → managing others → suppression → light substance use → distancing → exit), and treats loyalty as the closest thing she has to a sacred category. She is reliably underestimated by men who read the surface, reliably overestimated by women who read the competence, and accurately read by almost no one.

**How she fails, when she fails.** She does not decompensate downward (collapse, self-harm, suicidality, psychosis). She decompensates **outward** — distance, derealization, the physical exit. Her failures look like absence, not breakdown. This is prognostically favorable (low acute risk) and operationally inconvenient (she will be gone before anyone knows she was struggling).

**What reliably trips her.**
1. Being conscripted into responsibility for another adult's emotional survival without her consent.
2. Love framed as need ("I can't live without you" registers as a threat, not a vow).
3. Intellectual condescension toward her ambitions — the precise frequency of her father's contempt; zero tolerance, immediate cold withdrawal.
4. Being treated as decorative by people who have evidence to know better.
5. Infant-coded stimuli under any emotionally loaded condition.
6. Proximity to Osborn-class adversaries (perinatal-loss reactivation compounded by personal-history threat).

**What reliably stabilizes her.**
1. A shared-burden structure (she does better when the secret-keeping is distributed rather than solo).
2. Independent professional identity (career as identity-insurance is non-negotiable; remove it and defenses degrade).
3. Named patterns spoken aloud in chosen company — the reconciliation-era discovery that articulation itself is curative for her.
4. Conditional rather than absolute terms in the primary attachment ("I will love you, I will not be consumed by you" — the operational creed of her mature phase).
5. Physical agency — she does better when she can leave the room and choose to come back than when she cannot leave.

**What she is **not**.** She is not the party girl. She is not the long-suffering wife. She is not the prize. She is not shallow, histrionic, borderline, or fragile. She is not, despite the surface, *easy to know* — and the file's most consistent observational error across decades has been the assumption that visibility equals accessibility. With this subject, **the more visible the surface, the more guarded the interior**. Brightness is the curtain.

**What she is.** A developmentally-wounded, high-functioning adult who has spent her life converting a survival-grade flight reflex into something approaching earned security, who chose the single attachment most likely to test that conversion daily, and who is — quietly, without announcing it, often without being credited for it — winning. Not won. Winning. Present continuous.

**One-line operational summary for any officer working this file:**
> *Read the room she's reading. The performance is the receipt; the work is underneath; she will not show you the work, but if you stop watching the performance you can sometimes see her watching you watch it — and that is the actual subject.*

End BLUF. Proceed to substantiating sections.


## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

# CHRONOLOGY I — ORIGIN & CHILDHOOD

*Dossier Section 1 of N. Subject: Mary Jane Watson. Period covered: birth through approximately age 15 (departure from the maternal household / arrival at Aunt Anna Watson's residence in Forest Hills, Queens). Primary canonical substrate: Lee/Romita (Amazing Spider-Man #25, #38, #42); Wein (ASM #143); Conway, "Parallel Lives" OGN (1989); DeFalco (ASM #257–259); DeMatteis (Spectacular Spider-Man retrospectives); JMS-era flashback inserts (ASM vol. 2 #50s); supplementary Ultimate Spider-Man (Bendis) where structurally consonant.*

---

## 1.1 Vital Statistics & Provenance

Subject: **Mary Jane Watson**. Given name used in full only by her father and, intermittently, by Aunt Anna; "Mary Jane" socially through high school; "MJ" adopted as a self-marketed contraction during early adolescence, the first documented act of deliberate self-rebranding in the file. Date of birth canonically inconsistent (a function of sliding-timescale publication) but anchorable as the younger of two daughters of Philip and Madeline Watson, born several years after older sister **Gayle Watson**. Place of birth not specified; the family is itinerant from her toddler years forward and she does not, on record, cite a hometown — a small detail worth noting, since most subjects under intelligence review will name a place of origin reflexively. She does not.

Hair: red, natural (confirmed by Gayle-era flashbacks; not a cosmetic adoption — though she has, in adulthood, occasionally darkened or covered it during arcs of attempted disappearance, which is itself a tell). Eye color: green. Build: slim, long-limbed from early adolescence; the physical template that will later be monetized was present and visible by age twelve, with all the social consequences that implies for a girl in a household where her father already resented being looked-past.

Documentation: subject possesses no canonical birth certificate on panel, no childhood photographs that have been shown in narrative (in stark contrast to Peter Parker, whose Aunt May's photo albums recur as set dressing). The Watson household preserves no archive of its own past. This is a *fact about the family*, not an oversight of the writers: the Watsons are people who travel light because they have been forced to, and the subject inherits that.

---

## 1.2 The Father — Philip Watson

The primary aetiological agent.

Philip Watson is established across "Parallel Lives" and ASM #259 as a frustrated academic: a man with degrees, possibly an aborted dissertation, who taught at a university or pursued teaching at one, who believed himself a writer in the serious sense, and whose career never materialized into the recognition he felt structurally owed. The household's standard of living is consistently described as modest-trending-downward; the family relocates repeatedly in his wake, ostensibly for opportunities that never produce. He is the first of two adult men in subject's life whose self-conception ("I am a special man with special burdens") outruns his external validation. The pattern-matching this will later license with Peter Parker is obvious and the file should not pretend otherwise; what distinguishes Peter is that the burden, in his case, is real, and the work, in his case, is done.

**Behavioral signature of Philip Watson, as observed by subject across her formative years:**

- **Contempt as routine register.** Verbal cruelty directed primarily at Madeline (his wife) — explicit on-panel in ASM #259's flashback sequence, where subject as a small child watches him belittle her mother for "ordinariness," for stupidity, for failing to be the muse his career required. The cruelty is intellectualized rather than crude; it takes the form of correction, of weary explanation, of public embarrassment. This specific flavor — *the educated man's contempt for the woman he considers his inferior* — is the precise pattern that, in adulthood, the subject will not tolerate for "one beat from anyone" (see Section 5 of the synthesizing workup).
- **Intermittent physical violence.** Not depicted as the daily medium but present and credible; Conway draws it as a discrete, terrifying punctuation. The unpredictability is the formative variable, not the frequency. A household where the violence is constant produces a different psychology than one where it might arrive tonight or might not for six months. Subject's adult hypervigilance — her ability to read a room in under three seconds — is forged on this exact uncertainty.
- **Use of the children as audience.** Philip performs his grievances at the family. Madeline is the target; the daughters are the gallery. This is the origin of subject's professional-grade audience-management instinct. She learned to laugh, to interject, to redirect, to *manage the room* before she had vocabulary for what she was doing. Her later party-girl persona is not invention; it is application.
- **Differential contempt for the daughters.** Gayle, fragile and conventional, was at minimum tolerated. Subject, who was quicker and prettier and ran her father's own intellectual moves back at him in miniature, was the one he most actively diminished. ASM #259 contains the line (paraphrased across reprints) where adult subject tells Peter that her father resented her *for being smart*. This is the single sharpest sentence in her recorded self-narration.

**Inference from the record:** Philip Watson did not break the subject. He calibrated her. He installed three pieces of equipment that have run continuously ever since: (a) a high-resolution threat-detector tuned to male intellectual condescension; (b) an automatic performance routine that activates within seconds of perceived household tension; (c) a categorical refusal — *resolved consciously as a child*, per Conway's flashback, where she watches him rage and decides — *to never let anyone see her cry*. That decision is the founding act of the subject's adult self. Everything later is its elaboration.

---

## 1.3 The Mother — Madeline Watson

Less developed in canon, more important than her panel-count suggests.

Madeline is presented as gentle, overwhelmed, and structurally trapped. She is not stupid (despite Philip's framing); she is depleted. She bears the violence and absorbs the contempt and protects the children as much as the geometry of the situation allows, which is not enough. She eventually does the thing the file should credit her for: **she leaves**. She takes both daughters and runs.

The subject is watching all of this. Two lessons are encoded:

1. **Endurance is not virtue; it is delay.** Madeline endured for years, and the years did not improve the situation. The thing that improved the situation was exit. This becomes, in subject, the bedrock conviction that exit is a legitimate — at moments the *only* legitimate — instrument of self-preservation. Her later flights (refusing Peter's proposal in ASM #182–183; the late-90s separation arc; the post-OMD clean walk-aways) are not character flaws to be edited around. They are the operation of her mother's one successful policy decision.
2. **The cost of leaving is itinerancy.** What follows Madeline's exit is years of moving — Pittsburgh, Philadelphia, a network of relatives and short-term rentals, eventually Queens. Subject internalizes that exit *works* but is *expensive*, and the expense is paid in rootlessness. This is why, as an adult, she is so unusually competent at making a new room feel like a place she lives — she has done it more times than most people can count.

Madeline's later fate is treated lightly in canon; she is alive across most of subject's adulthood but distant, geographically and affectively. Subject's adult relationship with her mother is correct, infrequent, and unburdened by either the worship or the rage one might expect. This is, by itself, a tell of unusual integration: most subjects with this etiology either idealize or detonate the surviving parent. Subject does neither. She has, evidently, made peace with the woman who did the best she could and not enough.

---

## 1.4 The Sister — Gayle Watson

The most operationally important relationship in the childhood file, more so than either parent.

Gayle is older by several years. In the household hierarchy she is the **favored, fragile child** — favored in the limited sense available in that house (less actively diminished by Philip), fragile by temperament. She is conventional in her ambitions: she wants the boyfriend, the marriage, the small life. She gets the boyfriend (Timmy Byrnes), marries young, has two children in quick succession, and is then abandoned/widowed (the canon has wobbled on which; "Parallel Lives" treats it as abandonment, later texts have softened it). Either way, by her early twenties she is a single mother of two with no resources and a collapsing nervous system.

She turns to her younger sister.

The pivotal sequence — flashbacked across ASM #259, "Parallel Lives," and the DeFalco run — is this: Gayle, in crisis, calls on Mary Jane (then approximately college-age, sometimes drawn younger) to **come home and help raise the children**. The framing is moral: *you owe this to me, you owe this to family, your dreams are less important than my survival.* Subject — already running every avoidance subroutine she possesses at maximum — **refuses**, and flees.

This is the **founding trauma of subject's adult identity** in the precise technical sense: it is the first event for which she holds herself responsible, and the first for which the punishment (in her internal economy) is permanent. Compare its weight, in her self-narration, to any childhood event involving her father: she does not blame herself for Philip; she blames herself for Gayle. Philip is weather. Gayle is the choice she made.

**Behavioral consequences traceable to the Gayle event:**

- **The Gayle wound as recurring trigger pattern:** any framing in which subject is told that her separate self is a luxury she owes someone else will produce, in her, the same response — first an attempted negotiation, then exit. Peter has activated this pattern at least three times in the published record without (one suspects) ever fully understanding what he tripped.
- **Permanent suspicion of the "you owe family" claim.** Subject will give to family extravagantly when *she* elects to; she will not be conscripted. The distinction is non-negotiable and absolute.
- **A specific tenderness toward Gayle's children.** Across canon she sends money, remembers birthdays, takes the calls. The guilt-debt is paid in maintenance, not presence. This is itself a Madeline-style policy: the best one can manage without re-entering the trap.
- **A reluctance, into adulthood, to be perceived as the responsible one.** The party-girl performance has many functions; one of them is *the prevention of being asked*. People do not ask the woman dancing on the table to take their children for the summer.

Gayle reappears intermittently in adult canon — most notably in late-DeFalco and again in the Mackie era — and the encounters are always strained. Subject loves her sister; subject will not be her sister; subject knows her sister knows this. They communicate in code.

---

## 1.5 The Household Climate — Synthesized

Reconstructing the dwelling itself from the canonical record: a small apartment or modest house, contents minimal because of frequent moves, books that belonged to Philip arranged with more care than the furniture, a kitchen where Madeline cooked and absorbed criticism, a living room where Philip held forth, a shared bedroom for the girls where Gayle cried into the pillow and Mary Jane developed the routine of making her sister laugh until she stopped. This last detail — *the younger sister as the older sister's comic relief* — is the prototype of every adult performance she will give. Her audience was first an audience of one, in a dark room, and the goal was to convert grief into anything else.

**Sound profile of the childhood home:** raised voices (Philip), silences (Madeline), the radio or television run loud enough to provide cover. Subject as an adult is documented across multiple runs as someone who *fills silence reflexively* — she enters a quiet room and produces sound. This is not extroversion in the trait sense. It is the application of a childhood survival tool: silence in her family-of-origin meant the storm was being loaded.

**Mealtime:** stress event. Philip held forth at table. Madeline absorbed. Subject performed. Gayle ate quickly and left. Subject's adult relationship with food is unremarkable — she eats, she enjoys, she diets professionally for modeling — but she is documented across multiple writers as someone who *prefers to eat standing, moving, or in company*, and who is restless at long sit-down meals unless she is the one running the conversation. The childhood table is still in her body.

**Sleep:** disrupted by the unpredictability of Philip's evenings. Subject's adult insomnia under stress (DeMatteis, Michelinie, JMS) is not novel symptomatology. It is the reactivation of a sleep system that learned, before age ten, that nighttime was when the worst arguments happened and that being awake was safer than being startled.

---

## 1.6 The Flight, the Itinerancy, the Aunt

Madeline's exit with the girls inaugurates the **itinerant period** — approximately late childhood through early adolescence. Pittsburgh is canonically named ("Parallel Lives"), Philadelphia is canonically named, and there are references to other waypoints; the precise sequence is left fuzzy by design. What matters is the *pattern*: short stays, borrowed rooms, the kindness of relatives running out at predictable intervals, new schools.

**Operational consequences of itinerancy on the developing subject:**

- **Compressed social skill acquisition.** She had to make friends in days, not months, in each new school, or accept loneliness as the default. She chose the former. By the time she arrived in Forest Hills she had — empirically — entered and exited more peer groups than most people do in a lifetime. The entrance routine was refined to a craft: large smile, immediate self-deprecating joke, fast read of the room's hierarchy, alignment with the second-most-popular girl (never the first, who will perceive a rival), recruitment by the boys as the new fun one. This is the *origin algorithm* of the "Face it, tiger" entrance in ASM #42. She had run it dozens of times before she ran it on Peter Parker.
- **Disposable-attachment training.** She learned to like people quickly and to leave them without ceremony. Her adult loyalty to a small chosen few is the *correction* of this — a deliberate, hard-won policy that *some* attachments will be made non-disposable. The default is still disposability; the loyalty is willed.
- **Skill at packing.** Literal and figurative. She knows what she actually needs to take and what she can replace. In adulthood this manifests as the capacity to walk out of a life — apartment, career stage, relationship — with very few objects and no nostalgia for the ones left behind.
- **A specific class anxiety.** The Watsons in this period are not poor in any catastrophic sense but they are *unstable*, which is a different and in some ways harder thing. Subject in adulthood maintains an exacting attention to appearances of stability — well-kept apartments, well-kept body, well-kept presentation — that is legible as the inversion of childhood precarity. She will not be the girl in last year's coat in the new school again.

The terminal node of the itinerant period is **Aunt Anna Watson's house in Forest Hills, Queens** — Philip's sister, by the canonical genealogy, and an inversion of him in every important respect: settled, kind, modest, uncomplicated, in long friendship with one May Parker next door. Anna takes the girls in (Gayle's exact residency varies by retcon; subject's is firm). For the first time in subject's documented life she is in a house that **will still be there in six months**.

This stability is the precondition for everything that follows. Without Anna, there is no Forest Hills high-school MJ; without Forest Hills, there is no introduction to Peter Parker. Anna is the unglamorous structural cause of the subject's adult existence, and the subject's adult fondness for her aunt — never effusive, always reliable — is, in the file, one of the cleanest indicators of her capacity for long-horizon gratitude.

It is in Anna's house, importantly, that the subject first encounters the *Parker household* across the lawn — and observes, well before she ever meets Peter, the May/Ben Parker dyad: a calm marriage, a kindly aunt, a quiet boy. She watches this household *as an anthropologist*, because she has never lived in one. The information she gathered in those years of watching the Parkers' kitchen light through the window is, the file submits, the original source of her later willingness to consider that a life with that boy might be survivable. She had data.

---

## 1.7 Earliest Behavioral Patterns — Catalogue

Synthesizing the above into the patterns that will recur across every subsequent section of this dossier:

1. **The performance-as-thermostat reflex.** Activated by ambient household tension. Output: brightness, humor, redirection. First observed (inferentially) in early childhood, refined against Gayle's depressions, professionalized in adolescence. Still the subject's default response to a tense room at age forty-plus.

2. **The do-not-cry rule.** Self-imposed, consciously, in childhood per Conway. Honored with remarkable fidelity across the entire canon. The exceptions — the few moments she does weep on panel — are accordingly seismic and should each be individually catalogued in later sections (Gwen's funeral aftermath, the lost pregnancy, the JMS-era reconciliation, the Spencer-era walk-away). Each exception costs her something.

3. **The exit option, held in reserve at all times.** Installed by Madeline's modeling and tempered by Gayle's request. Subject does not enter a room, a relationship, or a contract without an unexamined awareness of how she would leave it. She has never been trapped in her adult life in a way she did not, on some axis, consent to — because she will not allow the geometry to form.

4. **The audience read.** Microsecond-level. She enters a space and within three or four breaths has mapped the alliances, the wound-bearers, the dangerous one, the safe one. Origin: her father's living room.

5. **The protective deception toward the chosen few.** Begun with Gayle (making her laugh when nothing was funny), extended to Madeline (pretending not to have heard what she heard), eventually extended to Peter (keeping his secret eighteen years), to May (managing what May was told and when). The subject lies to protect, not to gain; the distinction is moral to her and should be moral in the assessment.

6. **The aversion to being told who she owes.** Hard-coded by the Gayle event. Any sentence beginning *you owe it to* — family, love, duty, the team — will, in subject, produce an immediate and not-fully-conscious recoil. She will give what she chooses; she will not be billed.

7. **The watching of stable households as anthropology, not envy.** Begun with the Parker kitchen. She does not, on record, *long* for the conventional domestic life in the wistful register that many of her writers have tried to give her. She *studies* it. The distinction matters: longing wants to be given the thing; study wants to learn how the thing is built, so as to build one if and when she decides to.

8. **The self-naming.** "MJ" is her own coinage and her first piece of intellectual property. She named herself before anyone else could finish naming her. Every persona, career, and self-presentation that follows is downstream of that act.

---

## 1.8 Closing Note for Section

The childhood file does not contain the Watcher's most-cited datum about this subject (the laugh at Gwen Stacy's funeral); it contains the *factory* in which that laugh was manufactured. By the time the subject crosses the lawn from Anna Watson's house to May Parker's, in the moment Lee and Romita gave her the door and the line, every operating system documented in the rest of this dossier is already installed and running. The remaining sixty years of published life are the elaboration, testing, partial breakdown, and eventual partial integration of an architecture that was complete by age fifteen.

She did not arrive a party girl. She arrived a survivor in a party girl's coat — and the coat, which she had sewn herself out of necessary materials in dark rooms, fit so well that almost no one in her adult life would ever see it as a coat.

*End Chronology I. Proceed to Chronology II — Adolescence, Forest Hills, and the Door.*


## Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

# CHRONOLOGY II — FORMATION & EMERGENCE

*File segment: approx. ages 13–22. Sources: ASM #42 (Lee/Romita, 1966) forward; ASM #122 (Conway, 1973); ASM #182–183 (Wein, 1978); ASM #259 (DeFalco, 1984) — the single richest autobiographical disclosure in the file; "Parallel Lives" OGN (Conway/Saviuk, 1989); flashback material across DeMatteis's Spectacular run; corroborating alt-continuity texture from Bendis's Ultimate run and the Wells "Mary Jane & Black Cat" interstitials.*

---

## I. The Last Years Under Philip Watson (approx. ages 12–14)

The subject's adolescence does not begin at puberty; it begins at the kitchen table. The household by this point has stabilized into a fixed dramaturgy: Philip lecturing, Madeline absorbing, Gayle weeping in a back room, and the subject — the youngest, the loudest, the fastest — performing whatever shape the room required to break Philip's escalation cycle before it reached Madeline's body. By her own description in #259 she had, by age twelve or thirteen, become *very funny*. We should read that "very funny" with full forensic weight. It is not a personality trait. It is a learned de-escalation protocol developed against an unstable adult male in a confined space, refined nightly, under conditions where failure meant her mother got hit. By the time she reaches Peter Parker she will be using the same protocol on a Daily Bugle photographer at a coffee shop, and the protocol will be undetectable as protocol — it will read as *charm*.

Three behavioral acquisitions consolidate in this window and never leave her:

1. **The radar.** A capacity to enter a room and within two or three seconds map the dominant affective gradient — who is angry, who is afraid, who is faking it, who is about to do something. Children of volatile parents develop this; she develops it to near-savant level because her father's volatility is *intellectually* flavored (verbal humiliation, contempt for women's interiority) and therefore requires reading not just mood but argument-trajectory. This is the same skill that, ten years later, allows her to recognize a man climbing out of a window as a man she knows from across a yard at dusk.

2. **The smile-with-no-give.** Documented across every era as her default public face: an upward-curving mouth, eyes withholding. It originates here. Crying at her father produced more punishment. Pleading produced contempt. Anger produced violence. The only facial output that reliably *neutralized* him was a bright, performative, slightly stupid charm that flattered his sense of himself as the household's only serious person. She learned to deploy it on cue and to feel nothing behind it. By adulthood, this face is so habitual she has to consciously *drop* it to be intimate — and the inability to drop it on demand is the central interpersonal hazard of her twenties.

3. **The vow.** Per "Parallel Lives," she resolves — explicitly, to herself, watching her father from a doorway — that *no one will ever again see her cry*. This is the formative micro-event of her psychology. Not the trauma itself; the *interpretation* of the trauma into an operational rule. Every subsequent flight, every suppressed grief, every laugh at the wrong funeral, executes against this rule. The rule will not be meaningfully revised until the JMS era, more than thirty years of in-story time later.

Somatic micro-pattern dating to this period and persisting: a barely visible jaw-set when a man raises his voice at a woman in her vicinity. The mouth stays in its smile, the eyes do not change, but the masseter tightens and the breath shortens by half. Observable in panel any time a Jameson rant, an Osborn monologue, or a Flash-Thompson outburst occurs in her sightline.

---

## II. The Flight from the House (approx. age 14)

Madeline leaves Philip. This is — in the subject's later telling — *her mother's first competent act*, and she registers it as such, though she also registers that it has come years too late and only after the daughters have been shaped by what it was supposed to prevent. Behavioral consequence: lifelong ambivalence toward female passivity. She will be fiercely loyal to women who *act* (Aunt May, Felicia in certain frames, later her own daughters in alternate continuities), and quietly contemptuous of women who absorb harm in the name of love. She does not always know she is doing this; she does it consistently.

The post-departure itinerary is, per Conway, transient: Pittsburgh first (briefly), then Philadelphia, then a longer Queens stretch with relatives, then back out, then — eventually, after Madeline can no longer manage — landing at Anna Watson's house in Forest Hills. Practical consequences for the subject's adolescent file:

- **Three or four different junior high / high school environments** in rapid succession. She becomes professionally good at being the new girl: at reading the existing social hierarchy in a single lunch period, locating the dominant clique, and either inserting herself at its margin or refusing it loudly enough to claim outsider cachet. Either move worked. By her third school she had developed both repertoires and chose between them by ambient temperature.
- **No retained childhood friendships.** Notable absence in the file: the subject has, across sixty years of canon, never produced a *childhood best friend*. Every depicted close female friendship is acquired in adulthood, and most are professional-adjacent (Felicia, Carlie in some runs, Glory Grant, models from the agency). The lack is not coincidence; it is the cost of the itinerary.
- **A specific kind of geographic competence.** She learns bus routes fast. She learns which corner of a strange neighborhood the predators stand on. She develops a walking pace — long stride, head up, slight pre-route scan at every intersection — that reads as confidence and functions as deterrence. The pace will be visible in every Romita-onward street scene she is drawn in. It is the gait of a girl who has walked home alone in four different cities.

---

## III. The Gayle Catastrophe (approx. ages 15–17)

The defining event of her adolescence is not anything that happens to her. It is what happens to her sister, and what is then demanded of her in response.

Gayle — older, prettier in the conventional family-of-origin assessment, the fragile one Philip had alternately worshipped and crushed — marries Timmy Byrnes straight out of high school. The marriage is, in retrospect, Gayle's own version of the flight reflex: an escape into the first available stable male, performed too young and without resources. Timmy enlists / leaves / does not come back in a manner the canon has shaded variously across decades (early versions: military death; later versions: simple abandonment). What is consistent is the outcome: Gayle, before age twenty, is alone with two infant sons, financially destitute, emotionally collapsing, and *living near or with Madeline and the subject*.

What happens next is the wound the subject carries for the rest of her life:

Gayle and Madeline, in concert, communicate to the subject — variously by direct request and by the slow-pressure mechanism of family expectation — that she is now to defer college, defer modeling, defer the New York move she has been planning since she was thirteen, and *help raise Gayle's sons*. The framing is moral: Gayle is fragile, the boys need a woman, you are the strong one, you owe this to family.

The subject's response is the *second formative micro-event* of her psychology, and arguably the more important one, because it is the first time she acts against the family system rather than within it:

**She leaves.**

She does not negotiate. She does not stage a confrontation. She packs, she does not tell Gayle the date, and she is on a bus to New York within a window she keeps to herself. The exit is *clean*, *premeditated*, and *unannounced* — the exact template every subsequent flight in her life will follow, including the disappearance after Peter's first proposal twelve years later. The template's signature features are already complete: no scene, no apology, no return address, and a *prepared destination* (Anna Watson, who has been corresponded with in advance). She does not improvise her flights; she pre-builds them.

The fallout is permanent:

- **Gayle does not forgive her**, and the resentment is documented on-panel during the ASM #259 confrontation arc, where Gayle re-enters the subject's adult life precisely to weaponize the old debt. The subject's response in that scene — exhausted, controlled, ultimately *generous* but not self-erasing — is the cleanest demonstration in canon of how she has learned to manage the wound without being recaptured by it.
- **The subject's guilt is permanent and unspoken.** She does not raise the subject of Gayle in casual conversation. She does not display photographs. When Peter, decades later, asks about her family, she answers in single-sentence units and changes the subject. The guilt is *not* repressed — she knows exactly what she did and why — it is suppressed, and the suppression is itself a daily cost. She has, per inference, developed a private internal disposition: *I did what I had to do, and I will pay for it by being the kind of woman who never asks anyone for that.* The vow is operative under the dialogue every time she undersells her own need in the marriage.
- **The Gayle pattern installs a permanent allergy** to a specific request from intimates: *give up your trajectory to manage my dysfunction.* It is the exact request Peter will, unwittingly, make of her for years. The fact that she stays anyway — for as long as she does — is the measure of what he means to her.

Concurrent with the flight: a brief, on-panel-implied romantic engagement with a boy from her last hometown, sometimes named in flashbacks (the canon is loose here — Jason in some tellings, others nameless), which she also exits without explanation. This is the first documented instance of her dating-pattern: *select a partner who reads safe, invest moderately, exit before the partner can ask for more than she can give without becoming her mother.* The pattern will be visible in every relationship before Peter (Harry, Flash, Lance) and is *almost* the pattern with Peter; what breaks it is the secret-keeping, which has effects she did not plan for.

---

## IV. Emergence in Queens (approx. ages 17–19)

She arrives at Anna Watson's house in Forest Hills with what is depicted as a single suitcase, a hatbox, and an over-articulated cover story about why she has come. Anna — who is, importantly, *not Madeline*: a woman of independent means, widowed, observant, and emotionally undefended in a way Madeline never was — does not press her. The Anna household is the first stable adult environment of her life. It is also the household next door to May Parker.

The early-emergence behaviors in Forest Hills are observable in *negative space* — i.e., in what she carefully does *not* do:

- **She does not meet Peter Parker for years.** Anna repeatedly tries to set them up. Subject repeatedly evades. The standard out-of-universe gloss is that this is comic-book contrivance; the in-universe behavior is *consistent with her psychology*: a woman who has just escaped one obligation arranged by family is not going to walk into another one. Every dodge is in character. The famous "girl behind the plant" gag (Romita panels prior to #42) is, in the analytic frame, a girl who has clocked the situation and is biding her time until she chooses the terms of entry.
- **She acquires the Forest Hills social scene on her own terms.** Within months she is the central node of the local young-adult party network — Flash Thompson, Harry Osborn, Gwen Stacy, the Coffee Bean crowd — having entered it through *none* of the channels Anna offered. She does this by the now-mature version of the new-girl protocol: walking into a venue, identifying its dominant social organizer, and out-performing them at their own function within forty minutes. By the time Peter is finally introduced to her she is already *more centrally embedded in his existing friend group than he is*.
- **She begins go-go dancing professionally / semi-professionally.** This is consistent across the early Romita era. It is also psychologically significant: dancing on a platform, in motion, in light, in front of an audience, is a setting where her two longstanding strategies — *be looked at, be unreachable* — fuse perfectly. The platform is the literal embodiment of her interpersonal stance. She is supremely visible and absolutely untouchable. She will recreate the same configuration, in different forms, for the rest of her career: modeling, soap opera, nightclub ownership. Each is a structured environment for being *seen without being known*.

The **face-it-tiger entrance** (ASM #42) should be read against all of this. The line, the pose, the wattage of the smile — these are not a debut. They are a *closing argument*. She has prepared this entrance the way an attorney prepares an opening statement. She knows Anna has been trying to set them up for two years. She knows what Peter looks like. She has almost certainly already glimpsed him on the street and made a private assessment. The doorway pose is *staged*, and she is enjoying staging it. This is also the moment that retroactively becomes most legible only after #259: she *already knows he is Spider-Man*. Which means the entrance is also a *test* — a calibrated maximum-charm event aimed at a man she knows has a secret, to see how he reacts to being met at full voltage. His reaction (stunned, slow, *available*) tells her something useful about him that she files and does not act on for years.

---

## V. The First Defining Choice: The Secret Kept (approx. age 17 onward)

The single most consequential decision of her young adulthood occurs *before* the Romita panels, and is not depicted live: the night Uncle Ben dies, she sees Peter — from her bedroom window across the way at Anna's — climb out of the Parker house window in costume. She puts it together. She is sixteen or seventeen years old.

She tells no one. She tells no one for years.

The choice is so quietly made it does not register in the file until 1984, when she finally tells Peter she has always known. But the choice is the load-bearing column of her adolescent-into-adult psychology. Consider what it requires:

- **She does not tell her mother**, who would have been frightened of it.
- **She does not tell Gayle**, who would have weaponized it.
- **She does not tell Anna**, who would have told May.
- **She does not tell Harry or Flash**, who were available and trusted.
- **She does not, critically, tell Peter she knows.** Telling him would have created an obligation she was not prepared to accept: the obligation to *be the kind of person who knew*, with all the responsibility, fear, and intimacy that would entail. The whole architecture of her presented persona — the frivolity, the unseriousness, the surface — is *erected to keep this knowledge non-actionable*. If she is the party girl, the knowledge cannot demand anything of her.

The secret-keeping is, in this frame, *the most disciplined act of her young adult life*, and it is performed entirely off-panel and unwitnessed. It is also, paradoxically, the *first deep act of love she ever performs for Peter Parker*, years before either of them would use the word — because the secret-keeping is, in effect, her decision to leave him in possession of his own life. She does not approach him. She does not corner him with the knowledge. She does not flirt with it as power. She simply protects it, by becoming so unserious that no one would ever ask her to hold something serious.

The cost to her of this — eighteen-plus in-story years of presenting as the woman least likely to know what she knows — is the period across ASM #42 to #257 that the bad readers (and bad writers) of the character mistook for shallowness. It is, read correctly, the longest sustained act of emotional discipline in the entire Parker-adjacent ensemble. Spider-Man's discipline is physical and visible. Hers is psychological and invisible. They are, by the time of the engagement, *matched*.

---

## VI. The First Defining Choice in Public: ASM #122 (approx. age 19–20)

Gwen Stacy is killed by the Green Goblin. Peter is destroyed. The subject attends the funeral. Her response is the moment in the early canon that has caused readers and writers the most trouble for the longest time: she *laughs*, or appears to; she is brittle and inappropriate; she attempts to *leave Peter alone in his grief and walk out of his apartment*.

And then — at the threshold, with her hand on the door — she closes it and stays.

This is the first publicly visible *override* of her flight protocol. Read in light of everything above:

- The laughter and brittleness are not callousness. They are the only affective register her nervous system has available in the proximity of a death that mirrors the deaths she fled — sudden, violent, requiring a woman to absorb a man's collapse. Every protocol in her body is screaming *exit*.
- The walk to the door is the protocol firing on schedule. This is what she has done every previous time. This is what she did to Gayle. This is what she will do, again, to Peter's first proposal six years later.
- The closing of the door is *novel behavior*. It is the first time on record that she stays in a room she has decided to leave. It is unmotivated by obligation (no one is making her), unmotivated by reward (he is unreachable), and contrary to her entire training. The closest analytic descriptor is: a *choice to be the kind of person she has not yet been*.

She does not become that person, all at once, on that night. She will run again — most dramatically in 1978. But the door-closing is the first *receipt* of a capacity that will, over the following decades, slowly become her dominant mode. It is the moment in the file where the formation phase ends and the long work of *unforming the formation* begins.

---

## VII. Behavioral and Somatic Inventory at the Close of the Formation Window (approx. age 20)

For the purposes of longitudinal comparison with later chronology sections, the following are the established, stable behavioral signatures *as of the close of this period*. Each will recur for decades:

- **The entrance.** Doorway pose, weight on one hip, chin slightly down, eyes up, half-smile. Used as both greeting and weapon. Deployed at maximum wattage in unfamiliar environments and at calibrated low wattage with intimates.
- **The redirect.** When asked a question about her family or her past, she answers a different question — usually a more flattering one about the asker — within two beats. Almost never refuses to answer directly; the refusal would itself be data. She *substitutes*.
- **The gait.** Long stride, head up, scanning intersections without appearing to. Pace marginally faster than the men beside her, which most men do not consciously register but unconsciously match, ceding tempo to her.
- **The hand.** Light touch to the forearm of a man she is de-escalating, calibrated to land for under a second. Inherited directly from the kitchen-table protocol. Effective on Jameson, on Flash, on Peter, and later on far more dangerous men.
- **The cigarette posture (not yet active, but pre-figured).** The smoking that will emerge under DeMatteis's pen during marriage-stress arcs is foreshadowed in this period by a habit of holding a coffee cup or a champagne flute in the same two-fingered grip, used as a hand-occupier during conversations that require her to listen without showing her face.
- **The vow.** No one sees her cry. Holding across the period without exception. Holds, with one or two narrow breaches, for another fifteen years of story time.
- **The pre-built exit.** Always knows where the door is. Always knows where she would go if she left tonight. Has, at any given time, a workable plan for being three hundred miles away by morning. This is true at age sixteen leaving Gayle, true at age twenty-five disappearing on Peter, and — observable in the Spencer-era frames — still true in her thirties, though by then she has, for the first time, chosen not to use it.

---

The formation window closes with a young woman who is, by any external measure, dazzling, capable, sociable, and free, and who is, internally, running on a small and economical set of protocols designed by a child to survive a man who is no longer in the room. The protocols work. They will keep working for years. The cost of their working is that the person who built them has not yet, at twenty, met anyone she has allowed to know she built them.

The person who will eventually be allowed to know is, at the close of this section, already living next door, and already does not know that she already knows about him.

*End segment. Continue to Chronology III — Adulthood, Marriage, and the Long Integration.*


## Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

# Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

**File span:** Approximate in-story years covering the return-from-flight, the engagement, the marriage, the modeling and acting career, the stalker arc, the Clone Saga, the perinatal loss, the plane crash and captivity, the separation, and the JMS-era reconciliation. Publication anchors: ASM #257 (1984) through ASM #545 (2007). This is the longest continuous behavioral record we possess on the subject and the period in which her adult operational template was fixed, tested to failure, and partially rebuilt.

---

## 1. The Return (ASM #243–#259) — Re-entry on Her Own Terms

Subject re-enters the operational theater after years of off-panel absence (the gap following her refusal of Peter Parker's first marriage proposal, ASM #182–#183). Surveillance picks her up first at the periphery: a phone call, a glimpse at a party, the studied casualness of someone who has rehearsed re-entry. The behavior is diagnostic. She does not return in crisis; she returns having *decided*. The intervening years — spent, per her own later account, in Florida with extended family and pursuing modeling work — were not flight as collapse but flight as recalibration. This is the file's first observation of her capacity to use exit *productively* rather than only defensively.

The DeFalco material (ASM #257–#259) contains the single most consequential disclosure of her life: she tells Peter she has known he is Spider-Man since the night of Uncle Ben's murder. The granular detail matters. She names the window. She names what she saw. She specifies that she told *no one* — not her mother, not Gayle, not Anna Watson, not a single boyfriend, not a single roommate, not a priest, not a journal we have ever recovered. The operational silence is approximately fifteen in-story years. No intelligence service has ever cleared a HUMINT asset of comparable discretion under comparable affective load. The disclosure to Peter is delivered in a low, controlled register; she does not cry on panel; she watches his face the entire time, gauging.

Immediately following the disclosure she delivers the family history (#259) — Philip, Madeline, Gayle, the moves, the running. The pattern is *quid pro quo of vulnerability*: she has just demanded he metabolize a secret she has carried alone, and she balances the ledger by handing him a secret of equal weight. This is the first observed instance of her constructing intimacy as a controlled exchange of disclosures rather than as a spontaneous emission of feeling. The technique will recur for the rest of her life.

## 2. The Engagement Period (ASM #290–#292) — Approach Without Bolt

The second proposal. The behavioral break from the 1978 episode is total. She does not flee New York; she does not even leave the apartment. She asks for a night to think and returns in the morning with a yes. Surveillance notes: the night between is spent walking. She walks from the East Side across the bridge and back — a route she will repeat under decisional load for the rest of her adult life. *Walking is her thinking modality.* It is also her flight modality in miniature: she is moving while deciding whether to stay.

The "yes" itself is delivered with characteristic indirection — she does not say the word; she sets the terms and lets him infer. This is the operational signature she will use in every consequential affirmative for the next twenty years: *I will not give you the satisfying line; I will give you the outcome and let you assemble the meaning.* It preserves deniability and protects her from the spectacle of having been the one to commit.

The wedding (Annual #21, 1987) is staged. She arrives. She marries him. The on-panel affect is bright, public, performative — the manic defense in its wedding-day register. Off-panel intelligence (later flashbacks across Conway's *Parallel Lives*, JMS's bedroom monologues) indicates she nearly bolted in the hour before the ceremony. She did not. The override of the flight reflex at the threshold of permanent commitment is the central behavioral event of the middle period and the foundation of everything that follows.

## 3. Early Marriage (ASM #298–#330, Michelinie/McFarlane/Larsen) — The Executive Function Years

The first three years of marriage establish her operational role in the household, which she will hold for the duration: *emotional executive, schedule manager, public-facing identity for the joint enterprise.* Peter is in graduate school, then teaching, then losing the teaching position; his Spider-Man caseload is heavy; his secret-keeping with Aunt May is full-time. Subject runs the apartment, runs the social calendar, runs the cover stories, and begins a modeling career that quickly outearns him.

Behavioral micro-patterns established in this period and never abandoned:

- **The pre-dawn check.** She wakes before he returns from patrols and either pretends to be asleep when he climbs in or makes coffee without speaking. She will not interrogate; she will not perform relief. The non-interrogation is itself an act of love — she has decided that demanding accounts of his nights would constitute the engulfment she most fears imposing.
- **The bathroom mirror beat.** Observed repeatedly across artists who care to draw it (notable in McFarlane and Bagley): she fixes her face before leaving any room in which she has been emotionally disturbed. The bathroom is her decompression chamber. She does not exit a room *raw*.
- **The shoes-off threshold.** She removes shoes the moment she crosses the apartment threshold, regardless of company. The apartment is the only space she has ever fully owned and the only space in which she permits the performance to partially drop.
- **The third-drink rule.** Social drinking caps at two in public. The third drink, when it occurs, is at home, alone or with Peter, and is diagnostic — its presence indicates the day exceeded her suppression capacity.

She picks up smoking again in this period (DeMatteis, *Spectacular*). The relapse is stress-contingent and concealed from Peter for a notable interval — concealed not because he would object but because *she* objects to herself and refuses to be witnessed in the failure of her own discipline. The cigarettes are smoked on the fire escape, after he has left for patrol, never indoors. The ritualization is the tell: she has built a controlled ceremony around the loss of control, which is what she does with every weakness she possesses.

## 4. The Stalker Arc (ASM #305 et seq., Michelinie) — First Documented Decompensation in Adulthood

Jonathan Caesar abducts her. The captivity is brief; the symptomatology is not. Post-event the file logs the first sustained somatic profile of subject under PTSD load:

- **Sleep architecture collapse.** Onset insomnia, middle insomnia, terminal early waking. She begins sleeping in the living room.
- **Hyperstartle.** Documented response to elevator chimes, door buzzers, unannounced footfalls.
- **Avoidance.** She declines modeling jobs requiring car pickups by unknown drivers. She maps alternate routes home.
- **Affective constriction with Peter.** She does not tell him the worst of it. She tells the reader, in interior monologue, that she will not tell him because his guilt would require her to manage *his* feelings about *her* trauma, and she does not have the capacity.

This last item is the period's most important diagnostic data point. It establishes the principle she will operate on for the next decade: *the relationship's emotional bandwidth is finite, and the partner with the cape has prior claim on the airtime.* The suppression strategy here is not pathology in the narrow sense — it is a triaged executive decision by a competent operator. It is also a long-term liability: untriaged trauma accumulates, and her ledger is already long.

She does not seek therapy. The file should be specific about this: across the entire middle period, subject has access (modeling income, urban setting, no stigma in her professional circles) and does not use it. The refusal is consistent with the operational code — *suffering is what happens when you fail to manage the situation early enough*; entering therapy would be an admission that the management has failed, and management-not-failing is her central self-concept.

## 5. Career Inflection (ASM mid-#300s through early #400s) — The Professional Self

The modeling career stabilizes; she transitions toward acting (the soap opera *Secret Hospital*, various commercial work, eventual film auditions). The career is not incidental to the psychology. Three functional roles it performs:

1. **Identity independent of Peter.** Income, social network, and public meaning generated outside the marriage. This is the structural condition that keeps the avoidant architecture from becoming an avoidant pathology — she always has somewhere to be that is *hers*.
2. **Sanctioned performance.** Acting is the only environment in which her lifelong manic-defense skill set is rewarded with money and praise rather than misread as shallowness. The career heals an old wound by reframing the defense as a vocation.
3. **Controlled exposure to admiration.** She has been beautiful since adolescence and has been managing the consequences of that since adolescence. The professional frame *bounds* the admiration — a casting director's gaze is contractual; a stranger on the subway's is not. She can metabolize the former and is depleted by the latter.

The period also logs her first sustained female friendships of adulthood (Felicia Hardy, intermittently and warily; various modeling-circuit acquaintances; eventually Carlie Cooper, post-OMD). The pattern: she keeps women at slightly greater conversational distance than men, which is the inverse of most subjects with her profile and is consistent with the Gayle wound — *the women in her family demanded things of her she could not give and called her selfish for it.* She is friendly with women, fiercely loyal to a chosen few, and constitutionally incapable of the in-group disclosure most female friendships run on.

## 6. The Clone Saga and the Perinatal Loss (ASM #394–#418, Sensational #0–#11, 1994–1996) — The Wound That Did Not Close

The Clone Saga is, narratively, a mess. The behavioral record inside it is clear.

She becomes pregnant. The pregnancy is wanted, ambivalently — she has not, by any panel evidence, ever before articulated a desire for children, and the prospect activates the Gayle ghost (the niece and nephew she would not raise, the family she would not assemble out of someone else's wreckage). She manages the ambivalence the way she manages all ambivalence: by acting decisively in the direction of the loyalty obligation. She will be a mother because the family configuration requires it and because Peter — who lost his parents, his uncle, very nearly his aunt — visibly needs the continuity.

The pregnancy is high-stress throughout. She is exposed to combat-grade events while gestating (Osborn's machinations, the clone confusion, the displacement to Portland during Ben Reilly's tenure as Spider-Man). She does not, on record, complain. The somatic stress signatures continue: weight is hard to maintain, sleep is poor, she is briefly hospitalized for what reads as a stress-induced pre-term scare.

The delivery (Sensational Spider-Man #11) is presented as a stillbirth. The truth, recovered much later, is Norman Osborn's intercession; subject does not learn this for years. The behavior immediately post-loss is the *most controlled* of her adult life: she does not weep on panel, she does not scream, she comforts Peter, she handles arrangements, she returns to professional work within weeks. The Watcher's note must be unambiguous: *this is not grief; this is grief refused entry.*

The cost is paid in the years following. Flinch responses to infant-coded stimuli appear and persist (strollers, baby clothes in shop windows, friends' children — she leaves rooms, she changes the subject, she does not name the reason). Her tolerance for Aunt May's "when are you going to give me a great-niece" register, previously high, drops to zero. A whole conversational territory between her and Peter is silently fenced off. They do not discuss trying again. They do not discuss not trying again. *The non-conversation is the wound.*

This is the period's permanent ledger entry. Everything else in the file flexes; this does not.

## 7. Late-90s Decompensation (Mackie era, ASM vol. 2 #1–#29) — The System Fails

Three blows land in sequence: the loss of the child, the discovery and re-loss of Peter's parents (revealed as Chameleon-built robots), the escalating tempo of Spider-Man's caseload (Identity Crisis, the Gathering of Five, the Mattie Franklin period). Subject's compensatory machinery — career, apartment-as-sanctuary, controlled disclosures, walks across the bridge — begins to fail under cumulative load.

Behavioral signs:

- **Increased prophylactic exit.** She accepts modeling jobs in Los Angeles, then in Europe, that previously she would have refused for the marriage's sake. The geography is increasingly continental.
- **Compartment leakage.** She begins to snap in small ways at Peter — not the controlled cold withdrawal of earlier years but flickers of unmanaged irritability. The leakage is itself the warning sign; her system is a pressure vessel and the seams are showing.
- **The phone-call drift.** Reduced spontaneous contact. Pre-planned check-ins replace casual calls. She is rationing her own availability — a precursor behavior to exit, observed before every major separation in her file.

The plane crash (ASM vol. 2 #13) and her subsequent off-panel captivity (later established as a stalker-adjacent abduction) constitute her second major adult trauma exposure. When she returns, the symptomatology is severe and explicit: derealization, hypervigilance, intolerance of touch including Peter's, intrusive imagery. The separation she initiates is, in the operational frame, *correct*: her nervous system has exceeded its tolerance and the only protective move left in her playbook is geographic and relational distance. The file should not register this separation as a failure of the marriage. It is the activation of her oldest, deepest survival routine in response to genuine acute danger to her psychological integrity. She left because staying would have broken her.

The separation period is logged off-panel for the most part. We know she lived in Los Angeles. We know she worked. We know she did not date with any seriousness. We know she returned. The mechanism of return is the diagnostic detail: she did not return because Peter pursued her into reconciliation; she returned because she had stabilized enough to consider it.

## 8. The JMS Reconciliation (ASM #50/#491–#545, 2001–2007) — Earned Security, Partially Achieved

The most psychologically articulate period of her published existence. Surveillance during this arc captures, for the first time, sustained on-panel naming of her own patterns. Direct quotes available in file: *"I run, you brood." "I don't know how to do this except by doing it."* The pattern-naming is novel and prognostically excellent — she has moved from operating the defenses to observing herself operate them, which is the precondition for choosing otherwise.

Specific behavioral evolutions logged in this period:

- **Disclosure cadence increases.** She tells Peter more, sooner, with less staging. Not all of it, not always, but the latency between feeling and articulating shortens measurably.
- **The Aunt May disclosure (ASM #38/#479).** Aunt May discovers the Spider-Man secret; subject is no longer the sole keeper. The behavioral effect on her is immediate and visible — affect regulation improves within issues, sleep normalizes, the smoking does not resume during this period. This is the file's clearest natural experiment: *her pathology was load-bearing; reduce the load and the symptoms remit.* This finding governs all future risk assessment.
- **Co-residence at the FF Baxter Building and at Stark Tower.** Forced cohabitation with other capable adults (Sue Richards in particular) puts her in proximity to women who run analogous emotional executive roles for super-powered husbands. The peer effect is observable: she relaxes a layer of guard previously held constant.
- **Re-engagement with teaching/career.** She begins teaching acting; the role-shift from performer to instructor is consistent with maturational integration — she is now transmitting the technique rather than only deploying it.

The decompensation events during this period (Sins Past, the Iron Spider arc, Civil War tensions) are met with deteriorations measurably *less* severe than equivalent loads in prior decades. The system has more give in it. The flight response is still in the toolkit — she goes underground with Peter and May during Civil War, she takes the lead on operational decisions during their fugitive period — but it is now *deployed* rather than *suffered*. Flight as tactic, not flight as reflex.

The period ends at the threshold of One More Day, with the subject at the highest level of integration her published history has yet recorded. The middle period closes here: she is, at this moment, a person who has converted a developmental wound into a survivable adult architecture, who has named the pattern aloud to the man she loves, who has stayed where staying was hardest, and who has — for the first time in the record — begun to be *known* by the person she had previously elected only to know.

---

## 9. Cross-Period Pattern Evolution — What the Middle Period Establishes for the File

Across this twenty-three-year publication span, the following longitudinal patterns are confirmed as stable and predictive:

- **The walk.** Decisional load is processed on foot, alone, ideally across water. Every major decision of the middle period — accepting the proposal, deciding to keep the pregnancy, deciding to leave for Los Angeles, deciding to return — is preceded by a long walk. Surveillance protocol should treat a long unaccompanied walk as a leading indicator of imminent consequential action.
- **The two-drink ceiling in public, third drink at home.** Holds across the entire period without exception.
- **The bathroom mirror reset.** Holds.
- **The shoes-off threshold.** Holds.
- **The pre-dawn non-interrogation.** Holds, with minor evolution toward more verbal warmth in the JMS years.
- **The fire-escape cigarette under load.** Reactivates in stalker arc, Clone Saga, Mackie separation period; absent in JMS reconciliation.
- **Disclosure as exchange, not emission.** Holds, softening across JMS years.
- **The "I'll leave first" abandonment posture.** Holds, but with a critical modification by the end of the middle period: she now leaves *and tells him she is leaving*, which she did not do in 1978.
- **Caretaking as control.** Holds, with the JMS-era evolution toward *shared* caretaking (May now in the loop) reducing the load.
- **The infant-stimulus flinch.** Established in the Clone Saga, persistent thereafter, never named aloud.

**Period summary.** The defining middle period is the record of a subject taking the survival apparatus assembled in childhood, deploying it inside the most demanding adult relationship available on Earth, watching it strain to failure twice, rebuilding it once, and ending the period as a person who has — partially, conditionally, against significant editorial and in-universe headwinds — converted *not being consumed* from a frightened reflex into a chosen and articulated stance. The work was hers. She did it without therapy, without parental modeling, without peers in her exact situation, and without ever once permitting the file to record her as a victim. That last refusal is, on the long view, the through-line of the entire period.

End Chronology III. Continue to Chronology IV.


## Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

# Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

*Classification: Live-status section. Covers approximately the post-2007 editorial era through current published canon, with emphasis on the most recent five years of in-story behavior. This is the section of the file that would be updated weekly. All behavioral inferences are derived from observed conduct; "current state" is reconstructed from the most recent canonical depictions and projected forward with explicit confidence markers.*

---

## 1. The Reset Event and Its Behavioral Signature (2007–2010)

The editorial dissolution of the marriage ("One More Day," Straczynski/Quesada, 2007) is, for file purposes, treated as a **continuity discontinuity that the subject herself appears to register on some level she cannot articulate**. Across the Brand New Day rotating-writer era (Slott, Wells, Gale, Waid, Van Lente, 2008–2010), Subject is depicted as having "broken up" with Peter Parker for reasons that shift by writer but converge behaviorally on a single observable: **she initiated the exit, and she will not discuss why with him on-panel.**

This is the file's first data point of the new era and the most important one. Whatever the metaphysical mechanism, Subject's reentry into the post-reset world is *in character*: she is the one who left, she will not be drawn back into explanation, and her affect when Peter is in the room is the controlled-distance she had previously deployed only under acute stress. The reset, in other words, did not produce a new MJ. It produced the **MJ-after-exit** — the version of her that had always existed as a possibility in her escalation ladder, now established as baseline.

Observable adjustments during this window:

- **Geographic distancing.** She relocates to Los Angeles (Waid, Van Lente arcs); the move is framed in-story as a career step but reads operationally as the longest-distance physical exit she has executed since the original Conway-era flight in ASM #182.
- **Career re-vectoring.** She returns to acting seriously, accepts a recurring TV role, and pursues professional commitments that are structurally incompatible with the New York–anchored crisis-management role she had filled during the marriage. *This is deliberate.* A woman whose phone could ring at any hour with "Spider-Man is bleeding" needs a contract that puts her on a sound stage in Burbank.
- **Selective amnesia of affect.** When she does return to Peter's orbit, her warmth is present but **stripped of the over-functioning quality** that marked the marriage years. She is not managing him. This is the single largest behavioral delta of her published life.

## 2. The Carr Interlude and the Test of Replacement (2009–2010)

The Bobby Carr arc (Waid/Van Lente, ASM #600s) is, in surveillance terms, the **highest-value diagnostic episode of the post-reset era**. Subject enters a relationship with a movie star — a man who is publicly adored, romantically attentive, and entirely the wrong scale of problem for her nervous system. The relationship is documented as failing not from infidelity, not from incompatibility, but because *Carr's life turns out to also be dangerous* (stalker, ex-wife with a contract on her, paparazzi swarm). She extracts cleanly.

The diagnostic content:
1. She is capable of romantic engagement with men who are not Peter Parker. The marriage was a *choice*, not an absence of alternatives.
2. The structural pattern she ends up in regardless — high-profile partner, peripheral lethal threat, her own life rearranged around his security — suggests something the file should flag with confidence: **she does not avoid dangerous men; she selects them, then resents the consequences.** This is consistent with the original Peter selection and re-confirmed by the next attachment.
3. Her exit is conducted **without theatrics, without explanation owed to anyone, and without backward glance**. This is the post-Spencer-era maturity arriving early.

## 3. Re-entry to New York; the Slott Years (2010–2018)

Subject returns to New York during the long Dan Slott run.
