- *Anniversary behavior.* She remembers dates and does not mention them. The day Gwen died, the day the baby was lost, the day her father last hit her mother — these dates produce a quieter version of her that nobody around her knows to attribute to anything.
- *Delayed onset.* The actual grief response, when it arrives, arrives months late and in private. A song on the radio in a cab. A child in a grocery aisle. She cries in the cab; she leaves the grocery store. She does not speak of it after.
- *Resistance to ritual.* She dislikes funerals, memorials, candle-lightings — not from disrespect, but because the public form requires a public face she does not have for this affect. She will arrive, she will perform, she will leave early.

---

## 10. VICTORY

She is, perhaps surprisingly, *worse* at victory than at defeat.

- *Compulsive deflection of credit.* "Oh, it was the team / the script / the lighting / Peter." She redistributes praise faster than she receives it.
- *Brief, observable euphoria* — a real laugh, a spontaneous embrace — followed within hours by *suspicion*. She begins waiting for the cost. The childhood model: good things are precursors to retribution.
- *She celebrates physically rather than verbally.* Dances, cooks something elaborate, initiates sex, takes a long bath. The body metabolizes the win because the mouth has not been trained to.
- *She does not tell her sister.* Across the entire record, she conceals her wins from Gayle. The guilt-debt forbids the comparative.
- *Within seventy-two hours of any significant victory, she will pick a small fight with Peter.* This is consistent across eras and worth flagging. The fight restores the equilibrium her nervous system trusts: a manageable problem, in motion, on familiar ground.

---

## 11. DEFEAT

By contrast, defeat she is *expert* at.

- *Immediate operational mode.* What is the next call, the next audition, the next plane.
- *No visible self-pity.* She will not give the room the satisfaction. The father lives, still, in any room that might watch her lose.
- *Private rituals.* The long shower. The cigarette she said she had quit. The single glass of something dark, poured neatly.
- *Re-engagement within forty-eight hours.* She does not lie down with a loss for longer than two nights. The third morning she is up, dressed, and acting as if the loss has already been metabolized — which it has not, but the *acting as if* is itself a documented and effective coping strategy for her.
- *She is generous to others who lose.* Castmates passed over, friends dumped, Peter when he fails. Her capacity to hold someone else's defeat without minimizing it is among her finest traits and is, traceably, the thing she wishes someone had done for her at fifteen.

---

## 12. DECISION HABITS

- *Speed.* Fast decisions on low-stakes matters; slow decisions on high-stakes ones, often agonizingly so. She has learned that her fast-decision instinct is *flight-coded* and she has trained herself to delay the consequential ones.
- *She decides in motion.* Walks, drives, runs — the consequential decisions are not made at the kitchen table. The body has to be doing something while the mind sorts.
- *She decides at night.* Three a.m. is the documented hour. She makes the decision then and announces it in the morning fully formed; she does not, ever, *workshop* a major decision with another person before it is made. This is a vulnerability she is aware of and has not corrected.
- *She honors her own decisions almost without reversal.* Once stated, she executes. Reversals are rare and almost always triggered by new information rather than by persuasion.
- *Her one persuadable axis is the wellbeing of someone in Tier 4 or 5.* She will revisit a decision for Peter, for May, for a hypothetical child. She will not revisit one for her own comfort.

---

## 13. ROUTES, RITUALS, ROUTINES

- *Morning:* shower before coffee, always. Coffee black if alone, with cream if performing for company. News scanned on phone, never on television — television in the morning agitates her.
- *Commute:* walks when feasible, cabs when not. Avoids subways under load. Window seat, eyes out, headphones in, no music — the headphones are a *do-not-approach* signal, not an audio choice.
- *Workday:* punctual to a fault. Early by ten minutes to any audition, fifteen to any meeting where she expects condescension. The early arrival lets her own the room before the room can size her up.
- *Evening:* low light. She kills overhead lights within five minutes of returning home, replaces them with lamps. The lighting choice is consistent across every apartment she has ever lived in.
- *Weekly:* a single long phone call with Aunt May, when May is alive and accessible. A non-negotiable. She has rearranged shoots for it.
- *Annually:* she does not vacation easily. When she does, she goes to water — beaches, lakes, never mountains. Water is the consistent restorative environment in the file.

---

## 14. INTEGRATION NOTE

The Atlas above describes a person whose every micro-behavior is in service of a single, decades-old project: *to be present in her own life without being captured by it.* The tells are not symptoms of pathology; they are the operating signatures of a successful, ongoing, never-completed negotiation between a child who learned that visibility was dangerous and an adult who decided, against the odds, to love anyway. Read the file accordingly. The hair-coil, the lipstick check, the three-a.m. tea — these are not weaknesses. They are the load-bearing micro-architecture of a self that, against all developmental prediction, is still standing, still choosing, and still — when the room is right and the person across from her has earned it — capable of the rarest behavior in the entire file: stillness, without performance, in the company of another human being.

*End Behavioral Atlas. Cross-reference Section 6 (Relational Map) and Section 9 (Threat & Vulnerability Matrix) before operational use.*


## Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

# DAILY LIFE, ROUTINES & RITUALS

## Preamble — How to Read This Section

A surveillance log on this subject is deceptively easy to keep and almost impossible to interpret. She is one of the most *legible* targets a watcher will ever draw: she moves through public space, takes well-lit cabs, keeps a phone on her, eats in restaurants, performs her job in front of cameras for a living. There is little tradecraft. There is also, at the surface, almost nothing to find — and that is the point. The subject has constructed a daily life whose readability *is* the concealment. A week of footage will show you what she ate, who she kissed on the cheek, what nightclub she walked through at 1:14 AM. It will not, without careful longitudinal cross-reference, show you the three-minute pauses inside the day where her actual interior life happens. This file flags those.

Patterns below are aggregated across mainline canon from the Stone/Conway domestic era through the Michelinie/DeMatteis marriage years, the Mackie separation, the JMS reconciliation, and the post-OMD Slott/Spencer/Wells nightclub-and-independence period. Where eras diverge, divergence is noted.

---

## 1. The Wake Cycle

**Time of waking.** Erratic by the standards of most files, consistent by the standards of her own profession. Default wake is 8:40–9:20 AM on a non-call day; 4:15–5:00 AM on a shoot day; rolling 11:00 AM–noon when running a nightclub operation that closes at 4. She does not use a single alarm — she stacks two, set seven minutes apart, the second across the room. The seven-minute stack is observable across multiple residences and is a tell: it dates to the modeling-career period and is a behavioral residue of having once badly missed a Eugene Colan-shot editorial call in the early Michelinie years. She has not missed a call since.

**The first ninety seconds.** She does not check her phone before her feet hit the floor. This is a *deliberate* rule, articulated to Peter on at least one observable occasion ("if I look at it in bed I've already lost the day"). She sits on the edge of the mattress, both feet flat, and breathes through the nose for a count she does not visibly mark but which times consistently to roughly forty seconds. Then she stands. The behavior is so consistent across years that it qualifies as a ritual; she would deny it is one.

**Hydration first, caffeine second.** A glass of room-temperature water from a carafe she fills the night before. Then coffee — black if she is alone, with a splash of milk if Peter is in the apartment, because she takes a sip from his cup as a greeting and matches her preparation to his. The coffee-matching is one of the most reliable affection-tells in the entire file.

**Bathroom sequence.** Long shower (12–18 minutes), hot to the edge of her tolerance. She sings in the shower — observable through walls in three documented apartments — and the song selection is diagnostic. Show tunes and Motown when she is well; quieter, lyric-forward singer-songwriter material (the kind she would not admit to liking in public) when she is processing. Silence in the shower is the single most worrying weekly data point a watcher can record. Silence means she is rehearsing a difficult conversation in her head.

**Skin and face.** A methodical, almost professional regimen — she learned it from makeup artists across two decades of being made-up by other people for a living and now treats her own face as a job site. The sequence is invariant: cleanse, tone, eye cream applied with the ring finger only (the weakest finger, least pressure), moisturizer, SPF even indoors. Makeup application time varies inversely with how the day is going — fifteen minutes when she is steady, forty minutes when she is anxious and using the mirror as a holding environment. Watch for the forty-minute days.

**Wardrobe.** She decides the night before and lays it out, a habit retained from her mother's coping behavior during the household-on-the-run years. Re-deciding in the morning is a stress signal. Color selection trends warm — rusts, greens, reds — and she avoids black on days she feels brittle (the inverse of what most people would predict; she does not want the camouflage, she wants the corrective). Heel height correlates inversely with how much standing-her-ground she expects to do that day: tall heels for press, flats for confrontations she has chosen.

---

## 2. The Morning Working Block

**Calendars.** Maintains a paper week-at-a-glance in addition to phone calendar. The paper version contains the actual schedule; the phone version contains what she is willing to be asked about. Cross-referencing the two is the single fastest way to map her real priorities. Color coding: ink only, no highlighters, blue for work, black for personal, red exclusively for Peter-adjacent items (medical appointments she has dragged him to, anniversaries, the date Uncle Ben died which she has tracked privately since adolescence).

**Email and message triage.** Twenty to thirty minutes, standing at a kitchen counter, never seated. Standing is deliberate — she does not want the inbox to feel like a place she lives. Responses are fast, warm, short, and consistently end with a piece of light social currency (a compliment, a tease, an emoji used as punctuation). She reads everything; she answers selectively; she lets a percentage of contact-attempts sit and decay deliberately, having learned that responsiveness is a form of consent she does not always wish to give.

**Agent / manager calls.** Standing rule: between 10:00 and 11:00 AM Eastern. She walks while on these calls — circuits of the apartment, never sitting — and ends each call by repeating back the action items in her own words. This is a habit picked up in her early acting career and never relinquished; she does not trust her own retention when emotional content is in the air, even professionally.

**Lines, scripts, prep.** When between productions: an hour of cold-reading aloud, alone, at the kitchen table, often material she has no intention of auditioning for. This is the closest she comes to a meditative practice. The performance-as-processing pattern is consistent with the broader psychological architecture — she metabolizes affect by routing it through a character.

---

## 3. The Body Block

**Movement.** Yoga (~four sessions/week), dance class (~two/week, jazz or ballet barre, depending on the city), occasional spin. She does not run. The non-running is notable for someone with otherwise high cardiovascular fitness; she has stated, in one observable conversation, that she "doesn't like the sound of [her] own breathing." Plausibly post-traumatic — sustained heavy breathing is a captivity-and-fear soundscape for her — and worth flagging.

**Studios.** Loyal to one yoga studio per city for years at a time, leaves abruptly when the social environment becomes inquisitive. Pattern across files: she stays at a studio until someone there figures out who her husband is or was, then transfers. She tips instructors generously on exit; she does not say goodbye.

**Eating.** Light breakfast (yogurt, fruit, sometimes a piece of dry toast — she is suspicious of butter in the morning, a leftover modeling-industry conditioning that she half-knows is irrational). Lunch is the meal she is most likely to skip, replaced by a third coffee. Dinner is the meal she takes seriously: she actually cooks, badly-but-improving, and the cooking is a deliberate domesticity-practice rather than a natural pleasure. Pasta is her safe range. She over-salts when distracted.

**The smoke.** Long-since-quit on paper. Still keeps a soft pack somewhere — top shelf of a kitchen cabinet, taped behind a bathroom panel, depending on residence — and a watcher will record, perhaps three times a year, a single cigarette smoked on a fire escape or balcony, at night, alone, in silence, never finished. The act is ritualized: she lights it, takes four or five draws, crushes it out, and stands for several minutes afterward with her arms wrapped around her own ribs. This is the body's grief-vent and she has not found a replacement for it.

**Drink.** Wine with dinner, two glasses at the high end, almost never spirits when alone. She drinks more in social-work contexts (openings, premieres, nightclub-floor passes) and the increase is performance-driven, not appetite-driven; she nurses, refills strategically, and is rarely actually drunk in public. Drunk MJ is a private event reserved for the apartment, post-fight or post-anniversary, perhaps twice a year.

---

## 4. The Workday Variants

**Shoot days.** Five AM call, car service, on set by six. She arrives with her own coffee and her own script copy even when both are provided. She introduces herself by first name to every crew member she has not previously met — grips, electricians, second-unit PAs, craft services — and remembers them on the next show. This is not pure warmth; it is also a tradecraft she developed in her twenties when she realized the crew tells the producers who is and isn't a problem. She is *never* a problem on set. Surveillance footage from any production she has worked on will show the same pattern: she is the last principal to complain about anything, the first to ask after a sick child of a crew member, and entirely capable of pulling rank with cold precision when the situation requires it (observed approximately once per production, always against a single specific offender, never broadcast).

**Press days.** Lower energy expenditure than shoot days, higher psychological cost. She runs a tighter ritual: arrives early, locks down a green-room corner, refuses to be staged in groups, drinks hot water with lemon between interviews. She gives reporters good copy — practiced, warm, quotable, deflective. She has a set of three or four anecdotes about her career she has told for years; the anecdotes are true but heavily edited. Anything personal she discloses in a press setting has been pre-cleared with herself, often weeks in advance. Spontaneous disclosure to a journalist is nearly nonexistent.

**Nightclub-operation days (post-OMD era).** Inversion of the schedule. Sleeps until 11, arrives at the venue by 2 PM, walks the floor with the day manager, checks stock, signs vendor paperwork in person rather than digitally (she trusts ink), tastes the kitchen, sits down with the booking calendar. The club is *her* space in a way her apartments often are not — she chose the lighting, the bar layout, the sound profile. She is observably more relaxed inside the club between 2 and 6 PM, before the staff arrives, than anywhere else in her week. She sometimes naps in the office between 6 and 8.

**Service hours at the club.** She is on the floor from approximately 10 PM until close. She works the room in slow loops, touches three to five regulars per loop, and maintains a mental ledger of who has had a bad week. She does not drink behind the bar; she drinks with selected guests at specific tables and the drinks are weak by her instruction. She handles disturbances personally — security is for show; her actual de-escalation tool is calibrated charm followed, if needed, by surgical coldness. She has thrown people out in her career and she does not raise her voice.

**Closing ritual.** After the last guest, she sits at the bar with the closing manager for fifteen minutes, going over the night. She tips out generously and in cash. She is in a car home by 4:00–4:30.

---

## 5. Domestic Patterns — The Apartment as Instrument

**Layout discipline.** Across every documented residence, the same spatial logic. There is a *public room* (living area, immaculate, performative) and a *private room* (bedroom or a small adjacent space) where actual living happens — script pages on the floor, mug on the windowsill, a single chair angled toward a window rather than a screen. Guests, including most of Peter's circle, are admitted only to the public room.

**Kitchen.** Functional, not gourmet. Salt, olive oil, three kinds of pasta, an actually-used cast-iron pan, an espresso setup that cost too much money. Refrigerator contents are perishable-light: she shops every two or three days at a neighborhood store where she is known by first name and where the staff have been instructed (by their own initiative, observably) to not make a fuss about her. She brings reusable bags. She tips the produce guy at Christmas.

**The bathroom.** The other psychologically loaded room. Three shelves of products; she uses perhaps a third of them and rotates the rest as moods change. The cabinet contains, longitudinally: ibuprofen, melatonin (used inconsistently), a sleep-aid prescription (used in arc-specific crisis windows only), magnesium, a hair-dye kit she uses herself rather than going to a salon when she wants to control timing. There is almost always a stash of Peter's things even in periods when they are not technically cohabiting — a toothbrush, a deodorant, a t-shirt folded with hers. A watcher who tracks the toothbrush count tracks the relationship status with high accuracy.

**Bedroom.** Made every morning. The bed-making is not housekeeping — it is closure on the previous day. She cannot start the morning's working block with the bed unmade. She has one specific pillow she travels with; she has had three iterations of essentially the same pillow over the years and replaces it before the previous one fully wears out. There is always a glass of water on her side of the bed. There is almost always a book, paper, often a paperback novel rather than something prestige-coded — she reads for escape and is unembarrassed about it in private and slightly embarrassed about it in public.

**Plants.** She keeps them, kills perhaps a third per year, replaces them. The replacement behavior is itself diagnostic: she does not accept the dead plant as a verdict on her capacity, she simply tries again. Apply this to the relationship pattern.

**Cleaning.** Tidies daily, deep-cleans in bursts, hires help intermittently and feels guilty about it. The guilt traces to having watched her mother clean without help; the hiring traces to having decided, in her thirties, that the guilt was a bad inheritance. She still strips her own bed.

---

## 6. The Phone, the Pocket, the Person

**Phone.** Always on her. Two devices in some eras (work and personal). She does not sleep with the phone in the bedroom — it charges in the kitchen, on a specific patch of counter, screen down. The screen-down rule is decades old and predates smartphones; in the early Michelinie years she did the same with a landline answering machine, turning the volume to zero overnight.

**Wallet contents.** Cards, license, modest cash. One folded item — varies by era — that she keeps without acknowledging: a ticket stub, a Polaroid corner, a note. The folded item is the closest she gets to a sentimental object she will admit to.

**Keys.** A small, tight ring. No keychain decoration. She has lost keys exactly often enough to have made a rule about it.

**Jewelry.** Minimalist working set: small earrings worn nearly every day, a thin chain, occasionally a ring on the right hand. The wedding ring's presence/absence across eras is a matter of historical record; the *replacement* objects she has worn in its absence (a different ring on the same finger, or a deliberately empty hand) are the more interesting tell. She does not wear pieces given to her by men she has stopped seeing. She does not throw them away either; there is a small velvet bag in a drawer somewhere.

---

## 7. Social Routines

**Friendships.** Maintained with discipline. She is the friend who calls on birthdays without prompting, who remembers the name of a friend's surgeon, who sends a card by mail in an era where almost no one does. The card-sending is calibrated: handwritten, brief, never effusive, signed simply. She has perhaps four to six people in her life at any given time whom she would call if something were genuinely wrong; the list shifts slowly over years. Aunt May, in her lifetime, was always on the list. Harry Osborn was on it once; the removal was permanent.

**Standing dates.** A weekly or near-weekly meal with a chosen intimate (in the JMS era and beyond, frequently May; in earlier eras, varies). The meal is a small ritual — same restaurant ranges, same approximate seating preferences (corner booth, back to the wall when possible, sight line to the door). The back-to-wall preference is not paranoia in her case; it is environmental control. She does not like to be approached from behind.

**Acquaintances and the public.** She is recognized in public with some frequency depending on era. Her response protocol is consistent: brief, warm, decisive exit. She signs an autograph if asked, declines a selfie politely about half the time, and never lingers. She has a specific small smile she deploys for strangers — close-lipped, eye-crinkle — that she does not use with intimates. Watch for the smile; it marks the social register.

---

## 8. Time with Peter — Micro-Rituals

When cohabiting or close-dating, observable rituals stabilize:

- **Morning coffee handoff.** Already noted; the matched preparation.
- **The door-touch.** She touches the doorframe with her left hand when entering the apartment if he is inside. She does not do this if he is not. It is so habitual she is unaware of it; a watcher will catch it on the third or fourth instance.
- **The phone read.** When his phone buzzes in the evening, she clocks the screen's reflection in any nearby surface rather than asking. She has trained herself not to ask. The asking would obligate him to lie and her to know. This is the entire secret-keeping architecture compressed into a household gesture.
- **The pre-leave check.** Before he leaves the apartment in the evening, she will, with high reliability, touch him — hand on the chest, hand on the cheek, a tug at a shirt collar. The touch is calibrated short and casual. It is also, on the longitudinal record, almost always the last thing she does before he goes out as Spider-Man. She does not say *be careful*. She has trained herself out of saying it; the touch replaces the sentence.
- **Return acknowledgment.** When he comes back, she does not immediately ask where he was or what happened. She waits. The wait is the gift she gives him and the discipline she imposes on herself. She will, however, look at his hands for damage before looking at his face. This is invariant.
- **The post-fight reset.** After serious conflict with Peter, she initiates physical re-contact within twenty-four hours, almost always by sharing food rather than by talking. The food is the apology; the conversation, when it comes, comes later and from him. She has gotten better at initiating the conversation herself in the post-OMD/Spencer-era data, but the food-first instinct persists.

---

## 9. Solitude — The Three-Minute Windows

The most important data in any week of surveillance on this subject is in the small gaps. Catalog the following:

- **The cab pause.** After getting into a cab or car service, she will look out the window without speaking for a stretch — typically two to four minutes — before engaging with the driver or her phone. This is the day's only consistent unstructured time. Her face during these pauses is the one she does not show anyone.
- **The elevator face.** Riding alone in an elevator, she lets the performance drop entirely. Mirrors catch this. The face is older, tired, attentive — the model-runner showing through the model.
- **The window stand.** Once or twice per day, she will stand at a window for between 90 seconds and three minutes, doing nothing visible. Arms usually crossed, weight on one hip, gaze middle-distance. This is the closest thing she has to a contemplative practice and she does not call it one.
- **The bath, occasional.** When badly stressed, an extra-long bath in the evening, lights low, no music. She does not read in the bath. She does not bring a drink. The bath is the body's permission to feel what the day did not allow.
- **The fire escape cigarette.** Already documented. Three or four times a year.

A watcher who catalogs only the public-facing day will write a file that says she is well. A watcher who catalogs the three-minute windows will write a file that says she is *managing*, which is closer to the truth.

---

## 10. The Sleep Architecture

**Falling asleep.** Difficult, historically. She reads until the book falls; she does not use screens in bed. Sleep onset is faster when Peter is present (observable across multiple cohabitation periods) and slower when he is out at night — she does not stay up *waiting* in any obvious way but the sleep latency data shows she also does not fully drop off until he is back in the building. This is not a choice; it is a body that has learned to track him.

**During the night.** Light sleeper. Wakes at unusual sounds; goes back down quickly if the source is identified. Wakes hard and stays awake if the source is not identified — and will then, calmly, get up and check the apartment herself rather than wake him. The self-checking is decades-deep behavior; she does not like having to be protected by being told to stay in bed.

**Nightmares.** Episodic, arc-contingent. Post-stillbirth and post-captivity periods showed a documented cluster of disturbed sleep; she has not, on the record, sought treatment for it, though in the JMS era she briefly engaged a therapist and then disengaged (the disengagement is consistent with the broader profile — she tolerates the work up to the point where it would require her to be the patient rather than the manager).

**Waking in the night.** If she cannot return to sleep, she does not lie still. She gets up, makes tea (chamomile, in a specific mug), takes it to the kitchen window, drinks it standing. She does not read at the kitchen window. She does not check her phone. She stands. She drinks the tea. She goes back to bed. The whole sequence runs about twenty-five minutes and is one of the cleanest, most repeated micro-rituals in her entire life.

---

## 11. The Week, Composite

A representative non-crisis week as a surveillance log would record it: four mornings with a yoga class or barre, two with extended phone-work at the kitchen counter, one slept-in. Two evenings on the club floor or at a work-adjacent event. One quiet dinner at home — cooked, two glasses of wine, music low, a paperback on the counter. One dinner out with Peter or a chosen intimate at one of three or four trusted restaurants. One night entirely alone in the apartment, by design, recovering. A bath. A window stand. Probably a cigarette, maybe not. Phone screen-down on the kitchen counter at 11:30 PM. Bed by midnight on the days she controls; bed by 4:30 on the days the work controls her. Bed made the next morning before the day begins.

A week reads as *enviable*. A year reads as *managed*. A decade reads as *the daily practice of a person who decided very young that the only way to survive being looked at was to design what was visible*. The dossier closes this section with the observation that the routines themselves are the achievement — not the romance, not the career, not the marriage. The achievement is that she built a livable day, every day, out of a childhood that should have produced almost any other kind of adult.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# UATU DOSSIER — SUBJECT: MARY JANE WATSON ("MJ")
## SECTION: RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

*Classification note.* This section maps the bonds the subject did not choose — the people who built the operating system before she was old enough to refuse the installation. Every later attachment in the file (Peter, May, Harry, Gwen, the children of alternate continuities) is legible only against these. The subject's adult relational signature — *warm-front, controlled-depth, exit before engulfment* — is reverse-engineered here, in the household on Wickham Street and the rented rooms after it.

---

### 1. PHILIP WATSON — Father. The Original Wound.

**Surface biography.** Academic manqué. A man who possessed the vocabulary of intellect without the temperament for the work — convinced he was owed a university chair, a published book, a public reputation; convinced the world (and specifically his wife) was the reason he had none. Sociology adjunct in the Conway "Parallel Lives" account; trailing teaching appointments through Pittsburgh and Philadelphia; an itinerant grievance.

**Behavioral footprint inside the household.** The dossier infers, from the subject's own later avoidance patterns and the few flashback panels permitted, a daily climate of *contempt as weather*. Philip did not, on the record, beat the children regularly; the violence is recorded as intermittent and primarily directed at Madeline. The damage was verbal and continuous — the curl of the lip at his wife's vocabulary, the public deflation of her at faculty parties, the diagnostic monologues delivered to a captive dinner table about why his ambitions were thwarted. The subject grew up in a room where one adult was always being slowly disassembled by another adult who believed himself the only mind present.

**The specific transferable injury.** Two distinct lesions, both still palpable in present-tense behavior:

1. *The contempt-pattern itself.* Intellectual condescension to women, particularly the framing of a woman's ambitions or pleasures as frivolous, registers in the subject as a one-strike trip-wire. She does not argue with it; she removes the speaker from her life. The cold surgical withdrawal noted under Pressure Points in the workup is this — Philip's voice coming out of a stranger's mouth, and the subject's nervous system executing the protocol it wrote at nine.
2. *The audience role.* Philip needed a listener. Gayle was too fragile, Madeline too beaten down; the subject — younger, brighter, faster — became the household's designated audience for the father's monologue, learning before puberty that her primary function in a room with a powerful male voice was to *receive*, to *respond appropriately*, and to *manage* the speaker's mood. The "party girl" extroversion later catalogued is not the opposite of this; it is the *promotion* of this skill from the kitchen to the discotheque.

**The non-cry vow.** Conway shows it explicitly: the child resolves, watching her father rage, that no one will ever see her cry again. This is not a metaphor in the file. It is a load-bearing operational rule that survives into the JMS era and arguably beyond. When the subject cries on panel in the adult canon (the rooftop with Peter, ASM #149; the hospital after the stillbirth; the JMS-era reconciliations), the moment is always staged behaviorally as *override* — the rule being broken — never as default release. Tear-shedding in her case is data, not noise.

**Trust / safety register.** Zero. Philip is the template of the unsafe person; subsequent males are evaluated in part by their distance from him.

**Failure mode.** The relationship has no functional present. Subject does not, across published canon, seek reconciliation, attend a funeral (where one is implied), or reference him by name with any warmth. The failure mode is *absence-as-policy* — the most coherent action available to her.

**Leverage (hostile-actor model).** Any operator wishing to destabilize the subject through the paternal channel would not invoke Philip directly (she has built immunities) but would *replicate his manner* in a trusted figure — a mentor, a director, a husband's colleague — and watch the subject's tells: the flat smile, the lowered shoulders, the sudden need to leave the room. The leverage is not Philip. The leverage is the *pattern* Philip carved, which can be activated by anyone fluent in his dialect.

---

### 2. MADELINE WATSON — Mother. The Cautionary Example.

**Surface biography.** Married young to a man who would come to despise her for not being his intellectual peer. Endured years of erosion. Eventually — and this is the single most important act in the subject's developmental record — *left*, taking both daughters, beginning the itinerant phase of the family's life. Died (per DeFalco and subsequent references) before the subject's adult stabilization, of unspecified causes that read in context as the body finally cashing in the bill.

**The double inheritance.** The subject's relationship to her mother is structurally split, and the split is diagnostic:

- *Madeline as model of escape.* She left. She took the daughters. She broke the household. The subject's entire adult exit-vocabulary — geographic flight, relational withdrawal, the refusal of Peter's first proposal, the disappearance in the late Conway run — is patterned on this maternal precedent. Whatever else Madeline failed to give her, she gave her the proof that *leaving was possible*. This is not trivial. Many daughters of contemptuous fathers never receive that proof and spend their lives marrying him.
- *Madeline as warning.* She also *stayed too long*. She arrived at the exit having already been hollowed out. She entered widowhood/independence without the resources — financial, emotional, somatic — to flourish in it, and she died early. The subject's secondary imperative, *do not be consumed*, takes its urgency from this. Madeline is the picture on the wall of what happens if you wait.

**Operational consequence.** The subject's threshold for exit is consequently set *low and early*. She would rather leave a relationship in year two with the wound still cauterizable than in year fifteen as her mother had to. Peter is the singular exception — the relationship she has consented to stay inside despite repeated trip-wire triggers — and the cost of that exception (the breakdowns, the smoking relapse, the plane-crash-era collapse) is itself the proof that her exit-threshold is normally that low.

**Affective residue.** Mixed. There is, audible in the rare references, a daughter's pity — the recognition that Madeline did her best with what was left of her. There is also, less audible but present in the avoidance of mother-coded language about herself, a refusal to *be* Madeline. The subject does not, even in the most domestic JMS-era panels, describe herself primarily in maternal terms. She is a wife, an actress, a partner, a friend; mother is a role she will perform when continuity asks but does not claim as identity. The vacancy is Madeline-shaped.

**Trust / safety register.** Posthumous. Trust in the abstract — *she tried* — combined with a permanent refusal to model her own life on the maternal template.

**Failure mode.** Insufficient maternal protection during the formative years. Madeline, herself under siege, could not shield the daughters from Philip's contempt; she could only eventually remove them from his proximity. The subject grew up learning that *even the protective parent does not protect in time* — and therefore that protection, ultimately, is a self-administered function.

**Leverage.** Any narrative or person framing the subject's life trajectory as *becoming her mother* — beaten down, defined by a difficult man, dying early of accumulated grief — produces immediate, visible compensatory behavior: a career push, a stylistic reinvention, a reassertion of independent income. This is a useful diagnostic tell more than an exploitable lever; the subject's response to the threat is constructive, not destructive.

---

### 3. GAYLE WATSON BYRNES — Older Sister. The Defining Guilt.

**Surface biography.** The favored child (favored by Madeline, in the post-departure household; tolerated by Philip as the fragile one). Married Timmy Byrnes, the high-school sweetheart, young and pregnant. Widowed or abandoned (canon has wobbled; the operative fact is *left alone*) with two small sons, Tommy and Kevin. Demanded, at the critical juncture, that the teenage subject sacrifice her own departure-from-the-house — college, modeling, the move to Aunt Anna's — to come help raise the boys.

**The fork in the road.** The subject refused. She went to Anna's in Queens. She began the life that became the published life. Gayle, in the original telling, took the refusal as betrayal and carried that grievance for years; the reconciliation in ASM #259 is partial and brittle.

**The structural function of Gayle in the subject's psychology.** Gayle is the *should-have-been*. Every time the subject has chosen her own life over a claim made on her by someone else's collapse, the Gayle template activates: the guilt-debt is paid down in micro-installments, never cleared. This is the single most important pattern to understand about the subject's adult relational economy — she *over-functions* for chosen intimates in part because she is permanently servicing the original debt to a sister she did not save.

**Specific operational effects:**

- The decades-long secret-keeping for Peter (the 18-year withholding of the Spider-Man knowledge) reads partly as *if I cannot rescue, I can at least not expose* — a Gayle-debt payment to a different person.
- The caretaking of Aunt May during the marriage era, beyond what was relationally required, reads similarly.
- The exit reflex, paradoxically, also traces here: Gayle's life is the worked example of *what happens if you let the claim land*. The subject's flight from engulfment is not only self-preservation; it is the refusal to repeat the trap Gayle fell into (the early marriage, the children, the abandonment, the collapse). She watched her sister get eaten by exactly this sequence and resolved never to enter it on those terms.

**Dynamics in adulthood.** Sporadic. The sisters are not estranged; they are not close. Contact tends to be event-driven (illness, family crisis, the rare holiday) rather than maintained. The asymmetry persists — Gayle still requires more than the subject can give without re-triggering the original engulfment fear; the subject still gives less than guilt tells her she should. The relationship is maintained at exactly the distance that keeps both functional.

**Trust / safety register.** Conditional. The subject trusts Gayle's *love* but not Gayle's *judgment about what the subject owes her*. The boundary is permanent.

**Failure mode.** Demand-overload. Any acute escalation of Gayle's needs — financial crisis, the boys in trouble, health emergency — will pull the subject across distance and into managerial mode, where she will over-perform and then withdraw to recover. The cycle has, per inference, repeated multiple times off-panel.

**Leverage.** Highest of any origin-bond. A hostile actor wishing to extract behavior from the subject would route through Gayle or the nephews. The Gayle-debt is the one ledger she cannot ignore.

---

### 4. TOMMY AND KEVIN BYRNES — Nephews. The Inherited Charges.

**Status.** Underutilized in canon, but present in the file as the *children she did not raise*. The boys are now adults in publication time; their lives have been left largely off-panel, which is itself a piece of evidence — the subject does not foreground them in her self-presentation, suggesting either guarded distance or active conflict-avoidance.

**Function in the subject's psyche.** They are the Gayle-debt made flesh. Every birthday card sent, every gift mailed, every awkward holiday phone call is a token payment on the original refusal. The subject's behavior toward them, where observable, is *attentive but non-claiming* — she gives without inserting herself into a maternal role she explicitly refused at the founding moment.

**Trust / safety register.** Affectionate, distanced.

**Failure mode.** If either nephew came to her in genuine crisis — adult crisis, the kind Gayle's life seemed designed to produce in the next generation — she would respond, and respond more than she could afford to, and resent it silently. The Gayle-pattern reproducing one generation down is the dossier's prediction for any future arc involving them.

**Leverage.** Secondary channel to the Gayle-debt. Less direct, equally exploitable.

---

### 5. ANNA WATSON — Aunt. The First Safe Adult.

**Surface biography.** Philip's sister (or sister-in-law, depending on the era's continuity); resident of Forest Hills, Queens; longtime friend of May Parker; the relative who took the subject in during the late-adolescent landing after the Madeline-led flight from Philip's household.

**The structural importance.** Anna is, on the record, the *first* adult in the subject's life who provided shelter without extracting performance. She did not require the subject to be the audience, the rescuer, the manager. She required only that she come home at a reasonable hour and be civil at the breakfast table. The subject's capacity, later in life, to *recognize* a safe environment when she finally enters one (the JMS-era equilibrium, the post-OMD chosen independence) is built on the proprioceptive memory of Anna's house. Without that calibration she would not know what safety felt like in her own body, and would not be able to identify it elsewhere.

**The matchmaking.** Anna and May colluded, gently and persistently, to put the subject in a room with Peter Parker. The operation succeeded on the third or fourth attempt (ASM #42). The subject, by the time of the famous entrance, had been hearing about Peter for months and had clocked enough adult subtext to know exactly what she was walking into; the door-frame pose and the prepared line are, read in context, a *preemptive seizure of the frame* by a young woman determined not to be set up like a parcel. She came in with the script already written. This is consistent with everything else in the file.

**Dynamics across the canon.** Warm, light, lifelong. Anna does not appear often in adult arcs but is referenced with consistent affection. The subject's behavior toward Anna is the closest thing in the file to *un-managed* relational presence — she does not have to perform for Anna because Anna never asked her to.

**Trust / safety register.** High. Possibly the highest in the origin column.

**Failure mode.** Limited exposure surface; Anna is not in a position to demand or wound. The only failure mode would be Anna's death, which would remove one of the subject's few un-effortful relationships and would, per pattern, produce a quiet, suppressed grief processed through increased activity rather than visible mourning.

**Leverage.** Low for hostile use; high for stabilizing use. A trusted figure routing communication through Anna, where Anna still lives, would be received with less defense than direct approach.

---

### 6. THE LOST CHILD — "Baby May." The Unburied.

**Status.** The full-term pregnancy of the Clone Saga era, terminated in the canonical text as a stillbirth, complicated by the meta-textual ambiguity (the Osborn-abduction reveal, the alternate-continuity survival in *Renew Your Vows*). For the subject's lived experience inside mainline canon, *the child died*. That is the datum her nervous system processed.

**Processing record.** Inadequate. The published canon shows her producing the controlled, productive, *suppressed* response catalogued in the Medical section — she got up, she went back to work, she managed Peter's grief alongside her own and arguably ahead of her own. There is no depicted arc of mourning that meets clinical adequacy. The loss was metabolized via the same suppression engine that has metabolized every other major insult to her psyche, and the bill remains unpaid.

**Behavioral residue.** Documented flinch from infant-coded stimuli without verbal acknowledgment of cause. A quietness in scenes involving other people's babies. A specific avoidance, across multiple writers, of the *name* — she will speak around the child rather than through her. The naming itself (May, for Aunt May) is an unprocessed knot: the lost daughter shares the name of the woman who became, post-loss, the subject's most stabilizing maternal figure. Any scene putting both names in the same room generates measurable subject discomfort.

**Trust / safety register.** Not applicable in the relational sense; applicable in the *self*-relational sense. The subject does not, on this matter, trust herself to look directly. She manages by oblique glance.

**Failure mode.** Reactivation. Any subsequent pregnancy, prolonged proximity to an infant, or Osborn-adjacent stimulus has the potential to crack the suppression and produce a delayed grief response of disproportionate magnitude. The dossier rates this as the *single highest unresolved vulnerability* in the subject's current psychological inventory.

**Leverage.** Catastrophic if accessed by a hostile actor with knowledge. The Osborn channel specifically — already historically weaponized — is the route by which this wound could be reopened with maximal damage. Any threat-modeling that does not flag this is incomplete.

---

### 7. THE GHOSTS IN THE FAMILY FRAME — Timmy Byrnes, the absent paternal grandparents, the half-mentioned cousins.

**Note.** The published canon is sparse on the extended Watson family. Timmy Byrnes (Gayle's husband) exists primarily as the *fact of his departure* — a second male abandonment in the family's intimate field, reinforcing the maternal-line lesson that the men do not stay. The paternal grandparents are ciphers; the Watson side beyond Philip and Anna is unmapped. This sparseness is itself a finding: the subject does not maintain, and does not appear to seek, a wider family network. The origin column terminates at the named figures above. There is no fall-back kin layer beneath them.

**Operational consequence.** When Anna goes, and when Gayle's claim eventually transmutes (through age, distance, or loss), the subject's origin-bond inventory will reduce to the dead and the chosen. The chosen-family column — Peter, May while she lived, Harry while he lived, the small handful of trusted others — will then have to carry the entire load. The subject is aware of this, in the implicit way she is aware of most structural facts about her own life, and it informs the unusual fierceness with which she protects the chosen bonds even when their costs are highest.

---

### CLOSING NOTE FOR THIS SECTION

The origin-bond column reads, in aggregate, as a *short list of intense entries with a thin bench*. One toxic father (avoidance-policy active), one tragic mother (template internalized, refused), one guilt-anchor sister (debt permanently serviced), two distant nephews (debt channel), one safe aunt (calibration reference), one unburied child (live wound), and a sparse periphery. The subject's adult relational architecture — the warm-front, the controlled depth, the low exit-threshold, the disproportionate loyalty to the chosen few — is not a personality. It is the only configuration that lets a person with this origin column stay in the world without being eaten by it. Every later relationship in this dossier is to be read against this map.

*End Section. Continues: Relationships II — Peter Parker, Primary Attachment.*


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

*Classification note. This section maps the load-bearing non-romantic attachments. Romantic partners (Parker, Jameson, Carr, Rabin, Thompson when relevant) and adversaries are filed separately. Family-of-origin (Philip, Madeline, Gayle, Aunt Anna) is filed under Relationships I. What follows is the chosen ledger — the people the Subject elected, in adult life, to admit past her warm-front. The list is shorter than her social surface suggests. This is itself diagnostic: the Subject runs an exceptionally wide acquaintance perimeter and an exceptionally narrow intimate core. Most names below are people she has bled for once and then quietly defended for the rest of her life.*

---

## 1. MAY PARKER ("Aunt May") — Surrogate Maternal Object, Eventual Confidante

**Provenance.** First substantive contact ASM #42 (Subject's introduction at May's house in Forest Hills). The pre-acquaintance is older: Aunt Anna Watson and May had agreed for years to introduce their respective wards, and the Subject grew up in adjacent kitchens hearing about "Peter" before meeting him. May, in other words, was *known of* before she was *known* — and known of *favorably*, as the woman Anna spoke of with warmth. This pre-loaded the relationship with positive valence the Subject's own home had never produced.

**Phase 1 — performance era (1966–c.1975).** The Subject manages May the way she manages every elder female: with theatrical sweetness, errands, compliments on hats, the ostentatious good-niece routine. May, who is no fool, reads it as charm and likes her anyway. Behavioral receipts: the Subject brings flowers unprompted, remembers May's medication schedule by ASM #50s, and on at least three documented hospital occasions sits longer with May than she sits with Peter. The notorious *failure to attend* (ASM #56) is the file's misleading data point — the Subject did not refuse to come; she came, looked through the door, and fled. This is the flight pattern, not indifference. She wrote May a letter she never sent (DeFalco backstory, ASM #259-adjacent).

**Phase 2 — quiet alliance (late 70s–80s).** While the Subject is in flight from Peter (post-ASM #183), she keeps contact with May. This is unusual and important. She telephones May on holidays; she sends postcards from modeling shoots; she visits Anna and "happens" to find May there. The relationship continues without Peter as the connecting tissue, which means May is, by this point, a primary attachment in her own right.

**Phase 3 — the secret-sharing pivot (JMS run, c.2005, ASM #478/#37-vol.2 era).** When May learns Peter is Spider-Man, the Subject is *visibly relieved on panel* — exhaling, sagging, allowing herself to be held. This is the only documented instance in the canon of the Subject accepting maternal physical comfort without first deflecting it with a joke. The interpretive weight is enormous: May becomes the first person to whom the Subject can speak about Peter's life without translation or concealment. The conversations they have in the JMS run are the closest thing the Subject has to therapy on the record.

**Dynamics.** Asymmetrical-then-symmetrical. May begins as the recipient of the Subject's caretaking-as-control; she ends as a source of unconditional regard the Subject did not have to earn. May is one of the very few people who has seen the Subject cry without commentary afterwards (JMS ASM #50 vol.2, in the kitchen, off the central panel — a deliberate authorial gesture toward privacy).

**Trust / safety score.** Maximum among non-romantic ties. The Subject has, on the record, told May things she has not told Peter — specifically, the texture of her father's contempt, which Peter has only ever heard in summary.

**Failure mode.** Loss of May would be catastrophic and would almost certainly trigger the Subject's deepest flight reflex. The Clone-Saga "death" of May (later retconned) coincided with the Subject's most prolonged period of suppressive overfunctioning — she did not grieve, she organized. A real, irreversible loss would crack the suppression strategy in a way the perinatal loss did not, because May is the only adult who has ever functioned for her as a non-conditional mother.

**Leverage on the Subject.** Anyone who could credibly threaten May would obtain immediate compliance from the Subject — and she would conceal the compliance from Peter. This is the cleanest coercive vector in the entire file and the one the Osborn lineage has historically exploited.

---

## 2. ANNA WATSON — Architectural Aunt, Original Safe Harbor

**Provenance.** Maternal aunt; takes in Madeline and the girls during the late phase of the Watson-household collapse. The Forest Hills house is, functionally, the Subject's first non-volatile residence. Anna is a quiet, unflashy woman — a deliberate contrast to her niece's noise.

**Dynamics.** Anna is the prototype the Subject has spent her life trying *not* to be (small, retiring, unmarried, ordinary) and simultaneously the prototype she most respects (stable, kind, untheatrical, unbreakable). The ambivalence is unresolved and probably unresolvable. The Subject's adult horror of being "ordinary" is in part a horror of becoming Anna; the Subject's adult capacity for sustained loyalty is in part a thing Anna modeled for her.

**Behavioral receipts.** The Subject calls Anna on a regular schedule (DeFalco-era panels show the phone calls as habitual, not arc-driven). She sends money once she has it, framed as gifts so Anna will accept them. She defends Anna sharply against any teasing — one of the few topics on which she has no humor.

**Trust / safety score.** Very high, but emotionally bounded — the Subject does not bring her *current* problems to Anna; she brings her *competent self* to Anna as a kind of report card. Anna is the audience for whom the Subject performs *health*.

**Failure mode.** Anna's aging and eventual decline (handled lightly in canon, kept off-page) is one of the Subject's quiet running griefs. She will not discuss it.

**Leverage.** Lower than May — Anna lives further out of the threat-radius — but non-zero. More important: any disrespect to Anna from a person in the Subject's circle produces immediate, lasting, surgical withdrawal. Harry Osborn once made a careless joke about "the old lady's house" (Stern era, ambient panel) and the Subject's warmth toward him cooled by a measurable degree for the rest of that arc.

---

## 3. HARRY OSBORN — Brother-by-Circumstance, Cautionary Mirror

**Provenance.** Roommate-of-the-boyfriend; the Subject lives intermittently at the Osborn apartment during the late 60s/early 70s and becomes Harry's confidante during his first stimulant collapse (ASM #96–#98 era).

**Dynamics.** Harry is the Subject's first sustained exposure to a peer whose family wound was visibly worse than her own. The recognition is mutual and produces a sibling-bond that neither of them ever quite articulates. She manages his moods with the same machinery she developed for her father — but with Harry, the machinery is offered rather than extracted, and this is a corrective experience. He, in turn, is one of the very few people who saw her bored, tired, and unmade-up in the morning and did not require her to perform.

**Phase 2 — Harry's descent.** As Harry's Goblin pathology activates, the Subject's response is *not* flight, which is itself notable. She stays in his orbit longer than self-preservation would dictate, attempts to mediate between him and Peter, and is visibly destabilized by his death (DeMatteis, *Spectacular* #200). She smoked through the funeral week. This is a load-bearing loss the file has under-weighted historically.

**Phase 3 — post-resurrection and re-loss.** When Harry returns in the Brand New Day era, the Subject's affect toward him is guarded in a way it never was originally — she has learned that this man's pattern is to come back and then leave again, and she will not re-open the wound at full aperture.

**Trust / safety score.** Was maximum in Phase 1; permanently degraded to medium-high after Phase 2. She loves him; she does not trust the floor under him.

**Failure mode.** Harry is the model for how she now reads volatile-male-with-good-intentions: she will help, she will not save. This was learned in his blood.

**Leverage.** Historical only. A credible Harry-coded threat (a friend in chemical collapse, a Goblin-adjacent operator claiming reform) reactivates her caretaking circuitry faster than her current adult judgment can interdict.

---

## 4. LIZ ALLAN (later Allan-Osborn-Foswell) — Lateral Peer, Long-Game Friend

**Provenance.** High-school adjacent; consolidates into real friendship through the shared Harry orbit and persists for decades.

**Dynamics.** Liz is one of the very few women in the Subject's life who is neither competition nor audience. The relationship is laterally honest in a way most of the Subject's friendships are not — they have, on panel, exchanged blunt assessments of each other's men and survived the exchange. Liz is also the only woman besides May with whom the Subject has been observed in extended unguarded conversation (DeFalco-era kitchen scenes, Slott-era boardroom scenes).

**Behavioral receipts.** The Subject remembers Liz's son Normie's birthday without prompting. She has, twice on the record, declined social invitations to be available to Liz during Osborn-related crises. She does not flirt in Liz's husband-adjacent presence, ever — a deliberate self-policing the Subject does not extend to most settings.

**Trust / safety score.** High. Stable. One of the file's quietest and most durable ties.

**Failure mode.** Liz's eventual entanglement with Alchemax and Norman Osborn-adjacent business is a slow strain. The Subject has begun, in recent canon, to compartmentalize what she says around Liz — not because Liz is untrustworthy, but because Liz's professional surface is now porous to the Osborn apparatus. This is a sophisticated, sad calibration the Subject has made silently.

**Leverage.** Indirect. Pressure on Liz or Normie would route through the Subject's protective instinct toward both.

---

## 5. GLORY GRANT — Civilian Anchor, Bullpen Sister

**Provenance.** Met through the Bugle / Peter's orbit; becomes a roommate-tier friend during the Subject's modeling-era New York life.

**Dynamics.** Glory is a *normal life* anchor — a woman whose problems are rent, men, work, and laundry, in roughly that order. The Subject uses Glory's apartment, Glory's couch, and Glory's company as a periodic decompression chamber from the Parker household's tactical-operations climate. With Glory she is permitted to be small and tired.

**Behavioral receipts.** Wine nights. Borrowed clothes returned dry-cleaned. The Subject covers Glory's rent twice on the record without making it a thing.

**Trust / safety score.** Medium-high on personal matters; deliberately low on Peter-secret matters. Glory does not know, will not be told, and the Subject's protection of Glory's ignorance is itself an act of love — she is keeping Glory off the threat board.

**Failure mode.** None active. The friendship is robust precisely because it is bounded.

**Leverage.** Low-but-real. Glory's safety is non-trivial to the Subject.

---

## 6. FELICIA HARDY — Frenemy, Mirror, Reluctant Peer

**Provenance.** Initial contact frosty (the Subject is the woman Peter chose; Felicia is the woman he didn't). Relationship evolves through forced proximity over decades into something the file must classify as an *ally*, with caveats.

**Dynamics.** Felicia is the Subject's funhouse mirror — another woman who built a brilliant performance over a damaged paternal substrate, another woman whose primary defense is presentation. They recognize each other on sight and have, on the record, said cutting accurate things to each other that no one else in either woman's life would dare. The Subject does not *like* Felicia in a comfortable sense; she *respects* her in a way she respects almost no one.

**Behavioral receipts.** During the post-OMD years they cooperate operationally on Peter-adjacent problems more than once. Felicia has, twice, warned the Subject about specific threats without being asked. The Subject has, once, intervened to keep Felicia out of a setup. There is no acknowledgement after either incident.

**Trust / safety score.** Low on motive, high on competence. The Subject would never tell Felicia anything she did not want repeated, and would trust Felicia in a tactical emergency above almost anyone except Peter.

**Failure mode.** Sexual triangulation with Peter, historically and structurally. The Subject's containment of this is one of her most disciplined ongoing pieces of work.

**Leverage.** Felicia is one of very few people who could destabilize the Subject by *withholding* information she has — and she knows it. The relationship is therefore permanently transactional under a veneer of grudging warmth.

---

## 7. CARLIE COOPER — Brief Sister-in-Arms (Brand New Day era)

**Provenance.** Slott-era introduction; becomes the Subject's drinking-and-debrief friend in the immediate post-marriage-erasure period.

**Dynamics.** Brief but formative. Carlie is the first woman after the editorial reset to whom the Subject offers any real disclosure about Peter — and Carlie is herself dating Peter at the time, which gives the Subject the structural relief of *handing the burden off* to a peer who, briefly, becomes her stand-in. The behavior is unusual and reads as the Subject experimenting with not being the keeper-of-the-secret.

**Trust / safety score.** Medium and time-limited. When Carlie exited the orbit, the relationship cooled by attrition rather than rupture.

**Failure mode.** The relationship is structurally fragile because it was Peter-mediated; without Peter as the spine, the Subject did not have the energy to maintain it independently. A diagnostic data point about the Subject's capacity for *organic* female friendship outside the Parker gravity well: it is real but limited.

**Leverage.** Negligible now.

---

## 8. JILL STACY (and the residual Stacy network) — Inherited Kin

**Provenance.** Inherited from Peter's pre-Subject life; the Subject inherits Gwen's family the way one inherits a debt.

**Dynamics.** The Subject is uncommonly gracious with the Stacy line, given the structural awkwardness. With Jill specifically (DeFalco/Mackie era) she is correct, warm, and very lightly armored. She never permits herself to compete with Gwen's memory and never permits anyone to make her perform mourning for a woman she did not get to know.

**Behavioral receipts.** Christmas cards. Polite distance. No volunteered intimacy.

**Trust / safety score.** Low-to-medium; deliberately bounded. This is a relationship the Subject maintains out of respect for Peter's history rather than personal demand.

**Failure mode.** Any attempt by the Stacys to position her as Gwen's successor produces immediate cold withdrawal. She is nobody's replacement.

**Leverage.** Minimal.

---

## 9. THE NIGHTCLUB / HOSPITALITY NETWORK — Operational Allies

**Provenance.** Built during her club-ownership phase (Wells/Spencer-adjacent runs, also alluded to in earlier club-management arcs). Comprises bartenders, doormen, two co-managers, a publicist, a lawyer, and an accountant whose names rotate across writers but whose function is constant.

**Dynamics.** This is the Subject's first network she built *as principal* rather than as girlfriend/wife/model. The behavior here is revealing: she is a *good* boss — pays on time, defends staff against difficult patrons, remembers names and family details, runs the kind of low-turnover crew that signals managerial competence. She enforces a no-press, no-photo, no-cape policy on the premises; the staff is trained to identify and quietly extract trouble before it crystallizes.

**Trust / safety score.** Compartmentalized. None of them know the Peter dimension. All of them would take a punch for her on the business dimension.

**Failure mode.** Any costumed incident inside one of her venues breaks the firewall and forces her to choose between protecting the business or protecting Peter's secret. She has, historically, chosen Peter — and grieved the business quietly afterward.

**Leverage.** Threats to her staff register with her almost as strongly as threats to her family. This is a vulnerability the file should flag.

---

## 10. BOBBI MORSE / CAROL DANVERS / JESSICA DREW — Peripheral Hero-Wives-and-Sisters Network

**Provenance.** Acquaintances acquired through Avengers-adjacent social functions. Light contact, sustained over years.

**Dynamics.** These are women whose lives intersect the Subject's structurally — they understand what it is to wait, to lie at parties, to hear a news report and not flinch visibly. The Subject's interaction with them is brief, mutually respectful, and laterally informational. None of them is a confidante. All of them are *recognizers* — they see her, she sees them, no translation required.

**Behavioral receipts.** A handful of panel-incidental conversations across decades. The Subject is notably more relaxed in their company than she is at, e.g., a publishing party.

**Trust / safety score.** Low for disclosure, high for code-recognition.

**Failure mode.** None active.

**Leverage.** None.

---

## 11. THE MENTORS — Quiet Professional Hands

The Subject does not name mentors readily; she has had several and credits none. The file reconstructs them.

**11a. The acting coach (unnamed across multiple runs; first referenced DeFalco era, recurring through JMS).** An older woman, theater-trained, who taught the Subject that *playing a character who feels the thing* was a legitimate technique and not a moral failure. This single intervention may be the most important psychological event in the Subject's adult life. It legitimized her primary defense as a *craft*, which is what permitted her to develop it past the point of being merely defensive into the point of being income-generating, identity-stabilizing, and (under JMS) self-examining. The Subject still uses techniques this woman taught her in non-acting contexts — breath control before hard conversations, posture as mood-regulator, the small backstage ritual of touching the doorframe before entering a room she does not want to enter.

**Trust / leverage:** The Subject would not have permitted this woman near her interior on purpose; she absorbed her gratefully and at distance. If the coach is still living in any continuity, the Subject sends checks.

**11b. The early modeling-agency principal.** Taught the Subject the business mechanics of being looked at — contracts, rights, how to refuse a shoot, how to read a room of men who think they own her time. This was not affection; it was tradecraft. The Subject is, as a result, unusually difficult to exploit in commercial contexts. She reads contracts. She knows her own image-rights. She has fired representation.

**11c. Jonah Jameson (paradoxical).** Through Peter and through her own brief Bugle-orbit work, the Subject has had sustained low-grade exposure to Jameson and has, despite herself, learned things from him: how to weather public hostility without flinching, how to occupy a room by volume when subtlety fails, how to keep moving when criticized. She would never call him a mentor. He is one, in a narrow band. She is also one of very few people on whom Jameson's bluster does not land — she reads him as her father minus the cruelty, which makes him almost endearing and entirely manageable.

---

## 12. PROTÉGÉS — The Inverted Caretaking

The Subject takes on protégés rarely and reluctantly, because being needed is her allergen. When she does, the pattern is consistent: she selects young women who remind her of *Gayle as Gayle should have been allowed to be* — bright, ambitious, salvageable — and she invests in their *autonomy* rather than their dependence.

**12a. Young actresses and models (recurring background figures across the modeling-era and club-era runs).** The Subject is documented giving practical advice — agent referrals, contract warnings, the names of photographers to avoid being alone with. She does not collect their gratitude. She moves them along.

**12b. May "Mayday" Parker (MC2 continuity).** In the alternate where her daughter survives, the Subject's maternal pattern is precisely consistent with the above: protective, autonomy-prioritizing, secret-keeping when necessary, candid when possible. She is the parent her own mother could not be and the parent her father refused to be. The MC2 portrait is corroborative of the mainline psychological read — when given a child to raise rather than a partner to manage, the Subject's defenses reorganize around the child's separateness rather than the Subject's own.

**12c. Normie Osborn (intermittent).** Through Liz, the Subject has carried a low-level protective interest in Harry's son for decades. She is careful with him in a way that reads as paying a debt to Harry. The investment is small but real and would activate sharply if Normie were in clear danger.

---

## 13. GENERAL OBSERVATIONS ON THE CHOSEN-FAMILY LEDGER

**Selection criteria, derived.** The Subject admits to her intimate core only persons who satisfy at least two of the following: (a) demonstrated they will not weaponize her disclosures; (b) carry a comparable or greater wound and therefore cannot occupy a position of superior knowingness over hers; (c) require nothing from her she has not freely offered; (d) tolerate her exits without scoring them.

**Maintenance pattern.** She maintains her ties by *small reliable acts* (calls, cards, remembered dates, money sent without announcement) rather than by intensive presence. This is bandwidth-efficient and emotionally bounded and lets her sustain a wider chosen network than her interior availability would otherwise permit.

**Disclosure pattern.** She discloses in a strict hierarchy: May (full, post-JMS) → Peter (full, conditional on his capacity to receive it) → Anna (curated upward) → Liz / Felicia (tactical, never personal) → everyone else (warm-front only). She has never, on the record, drunkenly over-disclosed. She has never, on the record, *cried in front of more than one person at a time.*

**Failure modes, aggregated.** The chosen ledger's single greatest vulnerability is its compactness. The loss of May would crater her. The loss of May *and* Peter in proximity would activate the deepest version of the flight reflex and would likely produce a geographic exit from New York. Anyone modeling the Subject for coercive purposes should weight May at approximately 0.6 of total leverage available, Peter at 0.3, and everyone else combined at 0.1.

**Leverage map for protective purposes (inverse).** To *support* the Subject under load — which is this dossier's actual purpose — the operative interventions are: (i) reinforce May's safety perimeter; (ii) preserve her hospitality-network's comp


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# RELATIONSHIPS III — RIVALS, ENEMIES & LOVERS

Classification: Companion section to Relationships I (Family) and Relationships II (Friends & Professional). This file maps the adversarial and erotic axes of the subject's life — the people who have wanted to harm her, possess her, replace her, or have her, and the people she has, in turn, wanted to keep, escape from, defeat, or be undone by. The list is not symmetrical. Subject has had few true *rivals* in the conventional sense — her social architecture preempts most competition — but a great many enemies, most of them inherited from her primary attachment, and a small, highly informative roster of lovers whose distribution across her life reveals more about her than any direct testimony she has given.

---

## I. RIVALS

### Gwen Stacy

The foundational rival, and the one subject never actually competed with on live ground. By the time their social orbits intersected (ASM #42–#90), Gwen was already Peter Parker's serious attachment; subject was introduced *into* that triangle rather than as a contender for its apex. The observable behavior across ~50 issues is not romantic rivalry but something more interesting: subject reads Gwen accurately and almost immediately — registers her seriousness, her conventionality, her father-anchored stability, her incompatibility with Peter's actual life — and elects, with characteristic economy, to be Gwen's friend rather than her competitor. This is the *first major public deployment* of her social-performance apparatus in service of a long-game read: she perceives that Gwen will not survive proximity to Peter's life, and she does not need to act against her to obtain what she will obtain by waiting.

*History.* Group dating, double dates with Harry and Peter, the long mid-Lee/Romita run of café scenes and Coffee Bean tableaux. Subject is consistently positioned as the "wrong" girl and Gwen as the "right" one, which subject not only does not contest but actively reinforces — she performs frivolity *harder* in Gwen's presence, deferring the field. This reads, in the file, as both protective camouflage (don't let anyone, including Peter, see what is actually under the costume) and as a moral choice she has never been credited for: she chose not to take from Gwen what she could probably have taken.

*Dynamics.* Asymmetric. Gwen never identified subject as competition; subject always knew Gwen was the incumbent. There is no recorded instance of subject undermining Gwen, sabotaging the relationship, or seducing Peter behind Gwen's back. The closest she comes is the ambient flirtation that is her baseline operating mode with all men, which Gwen registered intermittently with mild jealousy and which Peter, characteristically, missed.

*Evolution.* Death of Gwen (ASM #121) ends the rivalry without resolving it and converts Gwen into something far more dangerous than a living competitor — a permanent unconditional standard against which subject would be measured by Peter, by Peter's social circle, by the readership-internal-to-the-universe (J. Jonah Jameson's mawkish editorials, May's wistful comparisons), and, in her worst hours, by herself. Subject's notorious inappropriate laughter at the funeral (ASM #122) is not callousness; it is the dissociative threat response of a person who has just understood that she now has to live inside Gwen's shadow forever, and whose oldest defense fires before her grief can.

*Trust / Safety.* While Gwen lived: high baseline trust, no perceived threat in either direction. Post-mortem: Gwen becomes the unsafe figure — not because of anything Gwen did, but because Peter's idealization of her becomes a third presence in every subsequent intimate moment with him. Subject has lived with this for the entirety of her adult attachment.

*Failure mode.* Gwen as memory is the one rival subject cannot defeat by being herself harder, because the contest is rigged: the dead win. Subject's response, over decades, has been to refuse the contest entirely — never to mention Gwen unprompted, never to ask Peter about her, never to seek the comparison. This is psychologically efficient and emotionally expensive.

*Leverage.* Anyone who weaponizes Gwen against subject — a clone, a resurrection, a Jackal scheme, an Osborn taunt — gets immediate, surgical, lasting withdrawal. The Clone Saga (1994–96) tested this comprehensively: the introduction of a living Gwen-shaped figure into the marriage was the closest thing to a true threat the relationship has sustained, and subject's response was to compartmentalize Peter's destabilization, manage the household, and quietly determine that if he chose the past she would not fight for the present. She would simply leave. This is the file's clearest read on how she handles competition: *she does not compete*. She withdraws the prize.

### Felicia Hardy (Black Cat)

The only living romantic rival of real weight, and the one against whom subject has demonstrated the most interesting behavior. Felicia entered Peter's life during subject's long absence (ASM #194 onward, Wolfman/DeFalco), conducted a substantial affair with him, and was — uniquely — drawn to Spider-Man specifically and uninterested in Peter Parker, which made her the structural inverse of subject (who knew the man and tolerated the costume). When subject returned, Felicia was still in the picture; the triangulation was active for years of publication time.

*History.* The crucial early data point: subject and Felicia have, on panel, sat across from each other and *read* each other in seconds. Felicia clocks that subject knows the secret and knows Peter as a person — both of which Felicia, at that time, did not. Subject clocks that Felicia loves a costume and not a man — which immediately defuses the threat, because subject understands Peter well enough to know the costume cannot hold him alone. Subsequent encounters across the Michelinie, DeMatteis, and JMS runs are consistently civil-to-warm on surface, with a current of mutual professional respect underneath.

*Dynamics.* Two highly intelligent women who recognized each other as the only people in the room running real models. Neither pretends with the other. Felicia's flirtation with Peter in subject's presence is met not with possessiveness but with a kind of amused, hand-on-hip tolerance — *go ahead, try* — because subject has correctly assessed the asymmetric advantage.

*Evolution.* Civil rivalry in marriage era; post-OMD, with the marriage erased, the dynamic re-symmetrizes — Felicia and Peter resume intermittent involvement, subject dates other men, and the two women interact across multiple arcs (Slott, Spencer) with continued mutual recognition. Subject has been depicted seeking Felicia out for information, for tactical cooperation around Peter's safety, and — in at least one Spencer-era beat — for what reads as a guarded approximation of friendship-among-rivals.

*Trust / Safety.* Moderate-high *operational* trust (Felicia is reliable in a crisis and protective of Peter), low *relational* trust (Felicia will, given an opening, take what subject has). Safety: high — Felicia is not cruel, does not weaponize her sexuality against subject, and observes a kind of thieves' courtesy.

*Failure mode.* Subject's failure mode here is not jealousy but its opposite: she will, under stress, *invite* Felicia in by stepping back, on the theory that if Peter chooses the other woman the question is answered.
