This is the avoidant-flight strategy dressed as generosity. She has done this at least twice on record.

*Leverage.* Felicia's lever on subject is not seduction of Peter but the threat of *being a better partner to him than subject can bear to be* — i.e., a woman who likes the danger, who can run alongside him in costume, who does not require him to be the civilian he can never fully be. This is the only competitive frame that has ever actually unsettled subject. Subject's counter-lever is her ownership of Peter Parker the man, which Felicia, even at her most evolved, has never quite acquired.

### Carlie Cooper

A lower-grade rival of the post-OMD period (Slott era, 2010–2012), introduced as Peter's "compatible" girlfriend in the editorially-mandated absence of the marriage. Subject's behavior toward Carlie is the diagnostic note: she is *polite*. Pointedly, frictionlessly, almost generously polite, in a register that anyone reading the file recognizes as the upper end of her social-performance apparatus deployed precisely to communicate, without saying so, that the rivalry is not real. Carlie is dating the Peter Parker who exists in subject's absence; subject does not contest the field she has voluntarily vacated. When Carlie eventually learns the secret and leaves Peter over it, subject's response is unsurprised and uncruel. *Failure mode*: none significant; this is the rival who never registered as one. *Leverage*: negligible. The file note is that subject's grace toward Carlie is itself a flex — she could afford it.

### The "Mary Janes" of the post-OMD dating roster (counter-rivals: the women Peter dated *as Peter*)

A category rather than a list. Subject's behavior toward each — Michele Gonzales, Mockingbird's brief flirtation, Anna Maria Marconi (during the Superior period, which is a special case), Cindy Moon (Silk) — follows a consistent algorithm. Civil, brief, observational. She is not threatened by women who are not also women he has *let know him*. The exception is Anna Maria, toward whom subject (in the few panels they shared) demonstrated something that read as protective concern — she had clocked, faster than the in-universe characters, that something was wrong with "Peter," and her instinct toward Anna Maria was to warn rather than compete.

### Sha Shan Nguyen, Debra Whitman, Betty Brant (legacy)

Each one a prior or peripheral Peter attachment whom subject inherited as social context. Subject's behavior toward all three across decades is courteous, low-friction, and unthreatened. Betty in particular becomes something close to a real friend across the JMS and Slott runs — the women who survived proximity to Peter Parker form a small unofficial sorority, and subject is its quietly senior member.

---

## II. ENEMIES

The defining feature of subject's enemies list: she did not earn it. With perhaps two exceptions, every adversary mapped below is an *inherited* enemy, acquired by virtue of attachment to Peter Parker. Subject's psychological response to this inheritance is one of the great underread features of her character — she has, across forty years of publication, never once on record blamed Peter for the danger. The cost-accounting she ran when she chose him already priced this in. What she has done instead is develop, by exposure, a hostage's working knowledge of villain typology that rivals any civilian in the Marvel universe.

### Norman Osborn (Green Goblin)

The apex enemy. The man who killed Gwen Stacy, who has tormented Peter across the entire run, and who — in the single most consequential adversarial encounter of subject's life — abducted her during her pregnancy, faked the loss of her child, and (per Mackie/late-90s continuity, partially walked back, partially upheld depending on era) is the in-universe author of the perinatal trauma that the medical file flags as an unresolved vulnerability.

*History.* First proximity during Harry Osborn's social circle in late-Lee/early-Conway era — subject would have encountered Norman as Harry's father at gatherings, registered him as a contemptuous and intimidating man (a *type* she had been calibrated to since childhood), and probably disliked him on contact for reasons she could not at the time articulate. Post-Gwen, the relationship is permanent enmity, made worse by the long Osborn-resurrection arc (Clone Saga onward), during which Osborn moves from background threat to active personal antagonist.

*Dynamics.* Osborn understands subject's value to Peter and has, repeatedly, targeted her specifically as the mechanism for hurting Peter — kidnap, surveillance, the pregnancy interference, the various "Goblin Nation" and "Red Goblin" arcs in which her safety is the lever. He has also, on the record, *spoken to her directly* in a register of intellectual contempt that maps with frightening precision onto her father's verbal register: the same dismissal of women's competence, the same performance of superior knowledge, the same pleasure in cruelty disguised as candor. This is not coincidence in the file's read; it is the reason Osborn registers in her nervous system as a uniquely intolerable enemy. He activates two distinct trauma circuits at once.

*Evolution.* From background social figure → killer of friend → personal stalker → author of perinatal loss → recurring intimate threat. The trajectory is uniformly downward. There is no version of this relationship that ever improves.

*Trust / Safety.* Zero. Negative. Subject has, in JMS-era and Spencer-era panels, demonstrated a startle response specific to Osborn's voice and to imagery associated with him (the glider, the mask, the laugh). This is the single clearest PTSD trigger on the file.

*Failure mode.* When Osborn is the active threat, subject's normal stress-escalation ladder collapses several rungs at once. She skips humor, skips management, skips intellectualization, and goes straight to operational coldness — she becomes very still, very controlled, and very willing to do whatever is required, including putting herself at risk in ways inconsistent with her usual self-preservation. This is the one enemy in whose presence her flight strategy reverses into a kind of locked-in confrontation. The file reads this as the Gayle-wound and the father-wound fusing: Osborn is the man she has decided she will not run from, because running from him would prove him right.

*Leverage.* Osborn's lever on her is total: her body, her family, her unborn. Her lever on him is, surprisingly, real — Osborn has demonstrated an obsessive fixation on subject as *Peter's prize*, which is exploitable. She has been used, with her consent, as bait. She is also one of the very few people whose direct verbal contempt for Osborn lands; she has the social-cognition to know exactly where to cut him.

### Harry Osborn (Green Goblin II)

The most psychologically complicated entry on the enemies list, because Harry is also a *friend* (see Relationships II) and a former dating-circle peer from the Lee/Romita years. The enmity is intermittent, illness-driven, and grief-laden on both sides.

*History.* Subject and Harry dated, briefly and unseriously, in the late 60s/early 70s (the famous engagement scare that was less an engagement than a Harry-misread). Subject was fond of Harry, recognized his fragility, and pulled back from the relationship precisely because she could see — with her usual accuracy — that he was a man who would need to be carried, and she had already done her time carrying a fragile sibling. The breakup is one of her cleanest exits and one of the kindest; she does not humiliate him, she simply does not stay.

*Dynamics.* When Harry is well, friend. When Harry is in Goblin-collapse (multiple arcs across Conway, DeFalco, DeMatteis, and the various resurrection storylines), enemy — and an enemy with the specific painful texture of being someone she has loved and still wishes well. Harry has, in Goblin states, threatened her directly and threatened Peter through her. She has, in turn, been one of the few people whose voice can occasionally pull him back from the edge.

*Evolution.* Friend → almost-boyfriend → friend's-spouse-and-coparent (Liz) → intermittent enemy → ghost. Across the post-OMD resurrections the relationship has cycled through these states repeatedly.

*Trust / Safety.* High baseline trust in Harry the man; zero trust in Harry the Goblin. Subject has learned to hold both simultaneously, which is the kind of cognitive bifurcation she is, by temperament and training, unusually good at.

*Failure mode.* Subject will, against her own safety calculus, attempt verbal de-escalation with Harry in Goblin states longer than she would with any other adversary. This is the one enemy for whom she breaks her own rules of disengagement. It has nearly cost her.

*Leverage.* Mutual and exploitative. Harry can wound her in ways no other adversary can because she still loves the friend underneath. She can reach Harry in ways no other person can because he still remembers, somewhere, the woman who treated him kindly when he was a boy nobody else saw.

### Eddie Brock (Venom)

A direct personal threat to subject in his original incarnation (Michelinie/McFarlane, ASM #298-onward), distinguished by the fact that Brock came to *her home* and confronted her face-to-face when Peter was absent — the famous panel where Venom appears in the apartment and subject does not run. The Michelinie text supplies the famous "you have the heart of a hunter" line; the *subtext* is that subject, faced with a creature designed to terrify, deployed a stillness her childhood had drilled into her. She did not give him the fear-response he wanted. He left.

*History.* Pure inherited enmity, peaked in late-80s/early-90s arcs, attenuated as Brock's character drifted toward anti-hero and then hero status. By the 2010s the relationship is closer to wary truce than enmity.

*Dynamics.* Asymmetric in a useful way: Brock's hatred is for Peter; subject is collateral, which means his behavior toward her, while terrifying, has never been intimately cruel in the Osborn register. He has even, on record, demonstrated a flicker of conscience around her.

*Trust / Safety.* Low-to-moderate. Subject does not trust Brock but does not register him in her body the way she registers Osborn.

*Failure mode.* The Venom-in-the-apartment incident is the diagnostic data point: subject's stress response to direct existential threat without time to plan is *freeze-and-perform-calm*, not flight. This worked once. It is not a strategy that scales.

*Leverage.* None meaningful in either direction at current canon.

### The Stalker (Jonathan Caesar)

One of the only *original* enemies on subject's file — acquired through her own career rather than Peter's — and therefore one of the most psychologically important. Caesar (Michelinie era, ASM #305-ish onward) was a wealthy fan whose obsession escalated through surveillance, harassment, and eventual abduction. The arc is significant because for the first time in canon, subject is the *target* of a sustained adversarial campaign that has nothing to do with Spider-Man, and her response reveals what her threat-management apparatus looks like when it is purely her own.

*History.* Escalating contact across months of publication time. Subject's initial response was minimization (defense 1: brightness up, deflection), followed by professional management (refusing his overtures politely), followed by operational counter-measures (security, schedule changes), followed by collapse (insomnia, weight loss, eventual breakdown), followed by survival of the abduction and a long tail of post-traumatic symptoms that the DeMatteis run carries forward.

*Dynamics.* Pure predator/prey, with the predator selected precisely because subject is who she is — beautiful, public, performatively warm in a way obsessives misread as personal. Caesar is the cost-of-business for the career she built as compensation for the family she escaped, which is a bitter loop the file should not soft-pedal.

*Trust / Safety.* Caesar specifically: zero. Caesar-as-type: subject has carried, since this arc, a permanent recalibration of her openness toward male fans, photographers, and admirers in professional contexts.

*Failure mode.* This arc is the closest subject has come, on record, to a full PTSD presentation. The pickup of smoking dates here. The insomnia patterns date here. Her threshold for re-entering modeling/acting work shifted permanently after this.

*Leverage.* Posthumous (Caesar dies in-arc): none. But the *category* of leverage — celebrity stalker / fixated admirer — remains a lifetime exposure she manages with practiced caution.

### Mysterio (Quentin Beck)

A specifically pertinent enemy because Mysterio's modus operandi is the manufacture of false reality — and subject is a person whose entire psychological architecture rests on the controlled management of presented reality. Beck has, across multiple arcs, targeted her with hallucinatory environments designed to break her perceptual grip. The file note: she is unusually *resistant* to this, because a person who has spent forty years distinguishing her presented self from her actual self has well-developed muscles for distinguishing real from staged. Mysterio is one of the few villains who has *underperformed* against her relative to expectation.

*Trust / Safety.* Operationally hostile, not personally fearsome.
*Failure mode.* None significant; she has out-performed her own baseline against him.
*Leverage.* Subject's resistance to illusion is a transferable asset that has not been fully recognized in-universe.

### Mephisto

Not a conventional enemy — a metaphysical one. The author of the One More Day erasure (Quesada, 2007), which removed the marriage from continuity. Subject's in-character knowledge of Mephisto is limited and intermittent across post-OMD canon, but in the few stories where she is given awareness, her response is unsurprising: she registers him as the largest version of the thing she has always known the universe contains — a being whose pleasure is the contemptuous reshaping of other people's lives. The father-pattern again, at cosmic scale.

*Failure mode.* None that is actionable; she cannot fight him.
*Leverage.* None. But it is worth flagging in this file that the metaphysical author of her greatest loss maps, archetypally, onto her oldest enemy: the contemptuous powerful man who rearranges women's lives for his own amusement. The reader of this file may draw their own conclusions about why subject's post-OMD characterization carries the specific texture of a woman who has, somewhere below conscious knowledge, *been here before*.

### Inherited rogues — short-form entries

**Kraven** — has hunted her by proxy; she registers him as a dangerous animal, not a personal cruelty. Manageable fear, not phobic.
**Chameleon** — particularly intimate violation: has impersonated Peter in her presence and (in one Mackie-era arc) attempted to deceive her sexually. This is the second-most personally violating enemy on the file after Osborn, and the reason subject has, since, a verification ritual with Peter (small private exchanges, voice-tells) she does not advertise.
**Doctor Octopus** — the Superior Spider-Man period is its own dossier; subject's encounters with Otto-in-Peter's-body produced the slow-burn unease that the file reads as her social cognition registering *something is wrong with this man* faster than the in-universe text gave her credit for.
**Hobgoblin (Roderick Kingsley / Ned Leeds)** — kidnapper; the Leeds-as-Hobgoblin arc carries the added wound of Betty Brant's husband being the threat, which subject managed with characteristic compartmentalization.
**Sin-Eater, Carrion, Vermin, the long parade of 80s/90s threats** — each one a brick in the wall of cumulative trauma exposure that the medical file totals as combat-comparable.
**Kingpin** — distant, structural enemy; respected as a force, not feared as a person.
**Kindred** — Spencer-era, Osborn-adjacent, activated all of the Osborn-flagged triggers and is filed accordingly.

---

## III. LOVERS

The list is shorter than the public mythology suggests, and its distribution is more diagnostic than its content. Subject's reputation as a romantic free agent is it


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# UATU DOSSIER — SUBJECT: MARY JANE WATSON
## Section 6: Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

Classification note: Subject possesses no superhuman physiology, no mutation, no mystical investiture, no acquired enhancement of statistically meaningful duration. The temptation in a file like this is to treat that as the end of the section. It is the *beginning*. Subject operates inside the most powered environment in the documented world — Manhattan, the household of a costumed combatant, the orbit of Osborn, Stark, Fisk, Octavius — and has survived it for the entire span of her adult life. That survival is not luck. It is a portfolio of capacities, each developed in response to a specific environmental pressure, each visible in the record, each with definable mechanism, range, and breaking point. We will enumerate them.

---

### 6.1 The Primary Faculty: Real-Time Social Modeling

**Mechanism.** Subject runs, more or less continuously, an inferential model of every other person in her immediate environment — affective state, likely next move, what they want from her, what they are concealing, what will set them off. The model is built from microbehavioral input (facial micro-expression, posture shift, prosodic change, eye-track, hand position, breath rhythm) and updated in real time. This is not a metaphor for "she is intuitive." It is the trained perceptual apparatus of a child who grew up reading a violent father's escalation curve to know when to clown, when to vanish, when to take Gayle into the next room.

**Range.** Effective at conversational distance and across a crowded room. Subject is documented, across the Romita and DeMatteis eras, clocking the temperature of parties as she enters — who is fighting, who is drunk, who is interested in whom — within seconds of crossing the threshold. The "face it, tiger" introduction (ASM #42) is, on re-read with the file in hand, not a flirtation; it is a *demonstration* — she has already run the model on Peter Parker before he has finished standing up.

**Resolution.** Extremely high on affect and motive; lower on technical content. She will know that Peter is lying about where he was; she will not always know what he was actually doing until she chooses to think about it. This selective resolution is itself a defense — the model is throttled so it does not deliver information she has elected not to have.

**Signature application.** The most consequential single deployment in canon: as a teenager, from a window in Forest Hills, observing Peter Parker exit the Parker home in costume on the night Uncle Ben died, and *correctly inferring not only the identity but the moral weight of the identity* — and then sitting on that inference for nearly two decades of in-story time (ASM #257). No other supporting character in the canon, including trained detectives, has matched this single act of social cognition.

**Limits.** The model does not work on herself. The faculty that reads other people's interiors with sniper accuracy turns opaque when aimed inward — see §3, self-modeling deficit. It also degrades under acute personal threat (kidnapping arcs, plane crash); the apparatus is designed for ambient social weather, not for combat decisions, and she knows it.

---

### 6.2 The Performance Apparatus: Persona as Tool

**Mechanism.** Subject can construct, inhabit, and discard a calibrated public self on demand. This is not acting in the theatrical-school sense (though she has that too — see 6.3); it is a more fundamental capacity to *be* whichever version of herself the situation requires, with full conviction, while a separate executive layer monitors the deployment. The "party girl" presentation of the late-60s/early-70s books is the textbook artifact. So is the dignified press-conference wife of the Michelinie era. So is the wry, boundaried club-owner of the post-OMD years.

**Growth curve.** Earliest documented deployments (pre-introduction; reconstructed through Conway's "Parallel Lives") are crude and continuous — the child-clown is *always on* because the household never granted off-duty status. By her early twenties (Romita-Conway era) the persona is sophisticated enough to fool Peter, Harry, Gwen, and most of the readership for a decade of publication. By the JMS run she has gained the capacity to *narrate* the persona as a persona — "I run, you brood" is a piece of meta-commentary no earlier version could have produced. By the Spencer run she deploys the persona surgically and briefly, and spends most of her on-panel time out of costume, so to speak. This is the trajectory of a tool moving from compulsion to instrument.

**Range.** Effective across the full social spectrum she has been observed in: Queens working-class, Manhattan high-fashion, film-industry power, super-community press-gaggle, hostage situations. She has held composure under Norman Osborn's direct gaze, which is a benchmark few non-powered humans pass.

**Limits.** The apparatus is metabolically expensive. Subject pays for prolonged deployment in insomnia, appetite loss, tobacco recruitment, and eventual exit-behavior — see §2 and §3. The persona also has a *deflection ceiling*: certain stimuli (her father's specific verbal pattern; engulfment-framed pleas; infant-coded cues post-stillbirth) punch through the apparatus instantly, and the persona offers her no defense against them — she goes cold, or she leaves.

---

### 6.3 Professional Skill Stack

The record documents at least four distinct trained competencies, each with its own ceiling.

**Acting.** Theatrical and screen training begun in the early-80s DeFalco run, refined through legitimate stage work (off-Broadway in the marriage era), and parlayed into a soap opera contract and intermittent film work. Subject is, by in-universe critical reception, competent-to-good rather than great. The relevant observation for this file is that her acting is *continuous with her persona apparatus* (6.2) and therefore the place where her two strongest faculties meet a paying audience. The career is psychologically load-bearing: it gives her an institutionally legitimate context in which to do, for money, the thing she has done for survival since age six.

**Modeling.** High-end commercial and editorial work, primarily marriage-era and post-OMD. Requires precise control of facial affect, body line under sustained discomfort, and the capacity to flirt with a camera held by a stranger for eight-hour shifts. Subject excels. The skill is downstream of 6.2.

**Business ownership and operation.** Post-OMD canon (Slott, Spencer) shows her running, successively, restaurant and nightclub operations at a level of competence the writers do not always pause to mark but the panels show clearly — staffing, vendor relations, permits, security calibration, crisis containment when costumed combatants come through the front window. This is the *managerial competence* the §3 file logged as a positive compensatory mechanism, formalized into an income stream.

**Languages and adjacent.** Functional conversational French (documented in modeling-era European-shoot panels). Reading-level Italian. No documented combat training of significance, though see 6.5.

---

### 6.4 Secret-Keeping as a Discrete Skill

Treated separately because it deserves it. The capacity to hold, for eighteen years of in-story time, a piece of information whose disclosure would alter every relationship in her life — including the relationship with the man it was about — is not a generic feature of personality. It is a specific operational skill, and subject has it at a level rivaled in the documented world by perhaps three or four professionals (intelligence-trained adults; not amateurs).

**Mechanism.** A combination of (a) the persona apparatus (6.2) absorbing the information into the public self so seamlessly that even she does not visibly carry it; (b) selective resolution on her social-modeling faculty (6.1) — she does not let herself fully *think* about what she knows in contexts where her face might give it; (c) a willingness to be misread, indefinitely, as shallow or frivolous, in order to preserve the secret's cover.

**Cost.** Significant. The §2 somatic signatures — the smoking, the insomnia, the appetite shifts — are the metabolic invoice for this skill running in the background for two decades. The skill is real; the price is real; both belong on the ledger.

---

### 6.5 Survival Capacity Under Direct Threat

Subject has been kidnapped, on the conservative count, more than a dozen times by adversaries including the Hobgoblin, Venom, Chameleon, Mysterio, and Norman Osborn personally. She has survived a plane crash with prolonged captivity. She has been stalked, assaulted in her home, and used as bait. *She has, in every documented case, lived.* This is not nothing. It is a survival skill set worth itemizing.

**Composure under restraint.** Subject does not, on the record, panic visibly when bound, masked, or held at weapon-point. She conserves breath, observes the room, identifies which of her captors is the unstable one, and modulates her speech to lower his arousal. This is the §3 social-modeling faculty (6.1) deployed in extremis, and it is the skill that has most often kept her alive long enough for rescue.

**Verbal de-escalation.** Documented across multiple hostage scenarios. Subject's tone in these moments is *not* the party-girl persona; it is a flatter, slower, lower-register voice that the file should note specifically — it is the voice she did not, as a child, get to use in her father's house, and she developed it later as a counter-pattern. It works on most non-psychotic captors.

**Threat triage.** She runs an accurate ranking of who in the room is the lethal actor and addresses that person first. Demonstrated repeatedly.

**Limits.** No hand-to-hand competence above untrained-civilian baseline despite environmental opportunity; subject has consistently declined martial training when Peter has, more than once, offered to arrange it. The decline is itself diagnostic — accepting the training would constitute admission of the threat profile she prefers to manage by other means. She owns no firearm on the record. Her physical-survival ceiling is therefore *the moment her words stop working*, and she knows where that line is. When it is crossed she becomes a passenger in events; this is the structural reason rescue is required.

---

### 6.6 The Domestic-Executive Function

A capacity the canon does not always name but the panels constantly show. Subject runs the household — financial flow, social calendar, May's care during the relevant arcs, Peter's cover stories, the laundry that has costume fibers in it, the landlord who must not see what came in through the window last night. This is project management at a level a competent nonprofit director would recognize, performed without staff, alongside a separate career, while concealing the existence of the project from everyone outside the dyad.

**Signature application.** The Michelinie-era stalker arc and the JMS-era Aunt-May-knows arc both show subject quietly load-balancing — moving May, briefing her, re-routing Peter, all while booking auditions. The work is invisible because she has made it invisible, which is the highest grade of executive function.

**Limits.** The function has no off switch except exit. She has not, on the record, learned to delegate inside the dyad until the post-OMD era, and even then incompletely. When the load exceeds capacity she does not ask for help; she removes herself from the system. This is the §3 escalation ladder cashing out at the operational layer.

---

### 6.7 The Naming Faculty (Late-Acquired)

Most recently developed and prognostically the most important. Beginning in the JMS run and consolidating through the Spencer run, subject demonstrates the capacity to *name her own behavioral patterns aloud in real time to the relevant other*. "I run, you brood." "I will love you, I will not be consumed by you." "I knew, the whole time, I knew." This is a skill, not a trait. It was not present in the Romita, Conway, Wein, DeFalco, Michelinie, or Mackie eras. It appeared, it stuck, and it has survived editorial discontinuity — every writer who picks her up post-2007, even the ones who do not understand her, leaves the naming faculty intact because the character now reads wrong without it.

**Mechanism.** Self-modeling, finally turned on. The §6.1 apparatus, retrained at considerable cost to operate on its operator.

**Range.** Limited to chosen intimates. She does not, and will not, name her patterns to strangers, press, or therapists on the record — the work has been done privately and the artifacts are visible only inside the dyad.

**Limits.** Still degrades under acute stress; under sufficient load she reverts to 6.2 (persona) and 6.5-adjacent exit. But the faculty is now stable enough that she returns to it after the storm, which earlier versions of her did not do.

---

### 6.8 Symptom-as-Signature

Every faculty above is also a wound, and the file is incomplete unless we name the equivalence.

- The **social-modeling sniper** is the child who learned to read her father's escalation curve. The accuracy is purchased with the developmental cost of never having been allowed to *not* read a room.
- The **persona apparatus** is the clown who kept the house tolerable for Gayle. The conviction with which it deploys is purchased with the chronic background question of which of the selves, if any, is the real one — a question she has only recently begun to answer.
- The **acting and modeling careers** are the persona apparatus monetized, which means her livelihood and her oldest defense are the same machine; threats to the career hit her at a depth that career-threats do not normally reach.
- The **secret-keeping skill** is the eighteen-year cost of believing, correctly given her data, that disclosure invites obligation she cannot survive.
- The **composure under restraint** is the child who resolved that no one would see her cry, generalized into adulthood and proof-tested by Norman Osborn.
- The **domestic-executive function** is the parentified older-younger sister who took over from a collapsing Gayle and a collapsed Madeline, given a household of her own to run.
- The **naming faculty** is the only one of her capacities that is *not* a sublimated wound — it is the first faculty she has developed as an adult, in conditions of relative safety, by choice. That is why it matters disproportionately to the prognosis.

The signature, taken whole: subject is a person whose every documented power is a survival adaptation that grew teeth. The file's operational conclusion is that her abilities and her injuries are the same set of facts viewed from two angles, and that the work of her adult life — visible in the record, accelerating in the most recent canon — has been to keep the teeth while reducing the bleeding.

End Section 6. Proceed to Section 7.


## Operational Profile & Tradecraft

# OPERATIONAL PROFILE & TRADECRAFT — SUBJECT: MARY JANE WATSON

*Classification: Section 6 of standing dossier. This section concerns HOW the subject operates — the mechanics of her decision-making, her practical methods, the infrastructure she has built around herself, and the granular tells by which her operations can be identified in the field. Tradecraft is used in the broad sense: the learned, repeatable procedures by which a person manages their environment, their finances, their information, and their exposure.*

---

## 6.1 DECISION-MAKING ARCHITECTURE

Subject runs a **two-stage decision process** that is consistent enough across the canon to be considered her operating system.

**Stage one — instantaneous social-cognitive read.** Subject enters any environment (room, conversation, call, set, party) and within seconds performs a fast scan: who is dominant, who is anxious, who is dangerous, who is being managed by whom, what the temperature is, what the threat vector is. This is involuntary and continuous — a developmental adaptation from a household where reading her father's mood at the door determined the next four hours. Confirmed across thousands of panels: she is almost never depicted as *surprised* by a person's emotional state. She is depicted as surprised by *events* (an explosion, a costumed assailant, a phone call) but the human element she has usually already clocked.

**Stage two — interval before action.** Between read and response, subject inserts a measurable beat. In the canonical art this is rendered as a single panel of held expression — eyes slightly narrowed, mouth neutral, a half-second before the brightness comes back on. Romita Sr., DeFalco/Frenz, Bagley, and Romita Jr. all draw this beat; it is one of the most consistent visual tics in her depiction. Operationally it is the moment she selects which version of herself to deploy. She has perhaps six or seven, and the selection is made on cost-benefit, not on mood.

The result is a person who **almost never reacts**. She *responds*, on a one-beat delay, with a chosen instrument. The handful of canonical moments where the beat is absent — laughing at Gwen's funeral, the silent close-the-door at the end of #122, the on-panel exits during the Mackie separation — are the diagnostically important ones because they are the moments her tradecraft *failed* and the underlying nervous system showed through.

**Decision criteria, ranked.** Reconstructed from observed choices, not from stated values:
1. *Will this preserve my exit?* — every commitment is silently audited for whether it forecloses her ability to leave.
2. *Will this protect the small set of people I have chosen?* — Peter, May, Anna, Gayle's children (in arcs where she reconnects), her daughter in alternate continuities.
3. *Will this keep the performance intact in front of audiences who matter to my career?*
4. *Will this be honest?* — a real criterion, but ranked fourth; she will sacrifice it to the first three and has, repeatedly.

---

## 6.2 IDENTITY MANAGEMENT & PERSONA DEPLOYMENT

Subject runs a portfolio of presentation modes, each tuned to a specific operating environment. These are not masks in the pathological sense; they are calibrated instruments she selects with conscious intent, the way a working professional selects a register of speech.

- **The Brightness.** Default public mode. High wattage, fast banter, body language open and forward-leaning, the trademark "tiger" register. Used in: clubs, sets, auditions, parties, first encounters, hostile press, and any environment where the cost of being read is higher than the cost of being underestimated. Energetic cost: high; she is visibly drained by it on the descent (DeMatteis frequently draws this — MJ closing the apartment door, leaning her forehead against it, the wattage gone).
- **The Manager.** Domestic mode with Peter, May, and trusted intimates. Lower amplitude, dryer humor, hands constantly in motion — making coffee, folding, straightening, packing, repairing. The activity is not anxiety; it is the medium through which she conducts emotional work. She delivers difficult content while doing a small task, because the task gives the listener something to look at other than her face.
- **The Professional.** Set / business mode. Clipped, precise, generous with crew, hard with executives. Knows everyone's name on a production within forty-eight hours. Notably *not* flirtatious in this register — a deliberate boundary, refined post-OMD, because she learned early in her modeling career that flirt-in-the-workroom costs more than it earns.
- **The Cold Front.** Withdrawal mode. Affect flattened, voice quieter than her baseline, sentences shorter. Deployed when a boundary has been violated; she does not raise her voice, she lowers it. The Bobby Carr arc and the late Spencer run both feature canonical examples. This mode is a warning to people who know her; people who don't, mistake it for calm.
- **The Quiet.** Reserved for Peter alone, late at night, post-crisis. Full availability, low performance. The rarest mode. By volume, perhaps two to three percent of her depicted screen time. It is the only mode in which she will state a need without first wrapping it in a joke.
- **The Exit Posture.** Pre-flight mode. Documented across the #182 disappearance, the Mackie separation, and several intermediate arcs. Behaviorally signified by: increased tidying of personal effects, financial check-ins (subject quietly verifies independent funds before leaving), a marked uptick in *kindness* to the person she is about to leave, and a final, characteristic on-panel beat of looking at the room from the doorway. The kindness is not manipulation; it is the discharge of guilt-debt before the act.

**Switching tells.** The transition between modes is visible to a careful observer: a held breath, a small smile that doesn't reach the eyes, a hand reaching for the hair (more on this in §6.10). For most onlookers the switch is invisible; for someone who has watched her for years, the switch is obvious and unmistakable. Peter has learned to read it. May, in JMS-era depictions, has also learned to read it. Almost no one else has.

---

## 6.3 INFORMATION DISCIPLINE

Subject is, by any reasonable standard, an **exceptional intelligence officer of her own life**. The 1984 reveal in ASM #259 — that she had known Peter's identity since the night Uncle Ben died, and had told *no one*, for what amounts to fifteen-plus in-story years — is not a writerly retcon to be admired; it is hard evidence of a person with industrial-grade compartmentation discipline.

**Compartmentation practice.** Subject runs at least four information compartments at any time:
1. Things known only to her.
2. Things known to her and Peter.
3. Things known to her, Peter, and one or two trusted others (May post-JMS, occasionally Felicia, occasionally Anna).
4. Things known publicly.

Movement of information across these boundaries is deliberate and one-directional. She does not leak. The few times she has disclosed across compartments under pressure (telling Harry, telling May before JMS resolved it, telling a therapist in alternate continuities) have been depicted as conscious, weighed, and immediately regretted-and-managed rather than impulsive.

**Cover discipline.** She is fluent in the practical deceptions necessary to live with a costumed vigilante: she answers the phone in his voice's absence, generates plausible explanations for missed engagements, manages Aunt May's questions with prepared, low-detail answers, handles press inquiries about her husband's "freelance photography" without ever volunteering a single contradiction-prone specific. Her covers are *thin and consistent* — the tradecraft principle that elaborate stories collapse and minimal stories survive.

**Counter-surveillance instincts.** Across the kidnapping arcs and especially post the Chameleon and stalker incidents, subject has developed observable counter-surveillance habits: she checks reflections in store windows, varies routes between apartment and frequent destinations, clocks the same face seen twice in a day, and — in post-stalker arcs — explicitly screens incoming mail and calls. These are not paranoid; they are calibrated and they save her life on multiple occasions.

**Digital hygiene (modern arcs).** Spencer/Wells-era depictions show her with multiple phone numbers, careful about photographs (she will physically reposition herself out of frame at clubs when a stranger raises a phone — a learned habit from years of being photographed without consent), and conservative about social media posting that geolocates Peter or any of the Parker associates. She knows her own face is searchable; she treats it as a vulnerability of his more than of hers.

---

## 6.4 FINANCIAL TRADECRAFT

This is one of the most consistently *underplayed* dimensions of the subject and one of the most important.

**Income streams across eras.** Modeling (steady, irregular, peak earnings late 80s/early 90s), commercial acting (high-margin, project-based), daytime soap acting ("Secret Hospital," recurring lead, salaried), feature film work (variable), nightclub ownership and management (Slott era — both a real income source and a status anchor), event hosting and brand work (Wells era). Across the canon she has at no point been financially dependent on Peter in any sustained way; in several long stretches she has been the household's primary earner.

**Spending pattern.** Subject's spending is *bimodal*. On herself, presentation-adjacent expenditures (wardrobe, hair, gym, skincare, the public face) are non-negotiable line items — these are operational expenses for her professional life and she does not flinch at the cost. On everything else, she is conservative bordering on frugal: she has been depicted across multiple eras keeping a careful, almost obsessive eye on the household ledger, and she does her own bookkeeping rather than outsourcing it. The frugality is a Pittsburgh-and-Queens habit, surviving the modeling money.

**The independent fund.** Inferable but not always on-panel: subject keeps liquid resources separate from any joint pool. The pre-Mackie separation arc shows her with the means to leave on short notice; the Spencer arc shows her with the means to walk out of a relationship and into her own life without a transitional period. This is not depicted as planning to leave Peter; it is depicted as *the operational baseline she has maintained since she was a teenager*, because the day her mother finally left her father, the money that bought the bus tickets mattered. She will never not have that money again.

**Major asset classes.** Wardrobe (working capital), a strong professional network (relational capital — agents, casting directors, club operators), and, in the later runs, ownership stakes in the venues she runs. She does not, as far as the canon shows, invest in markets; her money posture is *liquid and movable* rather than *compounded and rooted*. This is consistent with the exit-preservation imperative.

**Notable refusals.** Subject has, on multiple occasions, declined work offered specifically because of her association with Peter — magazine covers framed around "the wife of the photographer who got the Spider-Man shots," tabloid interviews — and has declined work on moral grounds (a recurring pattern of walking off projects she finds exploitative, even at financial cost). This is one of the few areas where Driver #4 (honesty) outranks the others.

---

## 6.5 PHYSICAL INFRASTRUCTURE & ROUTE PATTERNS

**Residential pattern.** Across the canon: Aunt Anna's house in Forest Hills (adolescence, with frequent disappearances of weeks-to-months); the Chelsea brownstone of the early marriage; the Bedford Towers apartment (Stark-arranged, JMS era); various crash-bases during separations (a small Soho space, friends' couches, hotel rooms managed on her own dime); post-OMD an apartment of her own — *this is the operational shift OMD enabled and which she has refused to surrender*. The pattern across decades: she will live with Peter; she will not live *only* with Peter. Even within shared residences she has consistently claimed a discrete personal space — a vanity, a corner, a closet, a desk — that is hers and is not entered.

**Route patterns.** New York-tight: she walks where most would cab, cabs where most would subway, subways when she is in a hurry or in costume-of-anonymity. She varies routes between work and home. She knows which bodegas stay open late on her blocks, which doormen will hold a package, which side streets a tail cannot follow without exposure. She has internalized the geography of Manhattan and Queens to the level of a working actor who has lived there for thirty years, because she is one.

**Anchor locations.** Across eras: a specific diner she returns to (varies by run, but the *pattern* of having one is constant — a place where the staff know her order and she can sit alone); a specific gym or dance studio (movement as regulation); the rooftop of whatever building she currently lives in (she goes up at night when she needs to think — first seen with Peter on Aunt Anna's stoop equivalent, codified across the Michelinie and JMS runs). Threat assessment: anyone who knows her well enough to know the anchor locations is a small set; anyone who wants to find her predictably should look there first.

**Travel posture.** Packs light, packs fast, and is depicted across multiple arcs going from "I need to leave" to "out the door with a bag" in well under an hour. The bag is pre-considered if not pre-packed; the documents are in a known drawer; the cash is in a known place. This is not paranoia. This is a woman whose family fled her father with whatever fit in the car.

---

## 6.6 RELATIONAL TRADECRAFT

**Confidant selection.** Extremely conservative. Across sixty years of in-universe time, the canonical full-trust set is perhaps five to seven people: Peter, May (post-JMS), Anna (early), Harry (specific compartments only, and a relationship she has revised over time), Felicia (an unusual case — limited trust but high operational rapport, see below), Gloria Grant (intermittent), in alternate continuities her daughter. New entrants to the full-trust set are admitted at the rate of approximately one per decade.

**Acquaintance management.** Generous, gracious, low-disclosure. She remembers names, asks after children, sends notes, attends weddings and funerals. This is genuine warmth running on a strict information diet — she gives a person every social courtesy and almost no biographical specifics. People routinely come away from extended conversations with her unable to state a single fact about her current life that they did not already know.

**The Felicia Hardy relationship as case study.** Subject and the costumed thief operate at a *peer* register that few others reach with her. Both are women who built personas to survive; both run accurate models of each other; both know the other knows. The result is a relationship of mutual professional respect with very limited intimacy — a working alliance in which information is exchanged sparingly and precisely. This is the model for how subject handles potentially-threatening female peers in general: identify, respect, calibrate, hold at one arm's length.

**Conflict tradecraft.** Subject does not escalate. She withdraws altitude (the Cold Front, §6.2), states the boundary once, clearly, and then enforces by absence. She does not relitigate. She does not deliver ultimatums she will not keep. This is one of the most consistent behavioral patterns in the file and the source of most of her conflicts with Peter, who escalates verbally and expects to be met in kind; she answers volume with quiet, which he reads as coldness and which is in fact the discharge of decision.

**The 'kindness on the way out' tell.** Already noted in §6.2 but operationally important enough to flag separately: when subject is about to end something — a relationship, a job, a friendship — there is a documented uptick in *generosity* toward the soon-to-be-departed in the preceding days. Compliments arrive. A favor is volunteered. A small gift appears. Then she is gone. Anyone who knows her well enough to spot the pattern can predict the exit by 24–72 hours.

---

## 6.7 OPERATIONAL USE OF THE BODY

Subject has, since adolescence, treated her physical appearance as operational infrastructure. This is not vanity in the dismissive sense; it is *tooling*.

**Appearance maintenance as discipline.** She works out regularly (dance-based — modern dance, yoga, occasional martial-arts conditioning in arcs where she is preparing for stunt work). She manages diet with the discipline of a working actor. She maintains the hair as a deliberate signature — the red is real, the cut is current, the styling time is non-negotiable before any high-stakes deployment.

**Wardrobe as toolkit.** Subject keeps a tightly curated wardrobe organized by deployment context. The same closet contains the audition uniform (specific, professional, color-managed), the club attire (operational seduction — used as a tool of attention-direction, not invitation), the home clothes (oversized, soft, almost monastic), and the *exit kit* (jeans, boots, jacket, the bag — the clothes she would leave in). The exit kit is depicted across multiple eras and is functionally identical: it is what her teenage self wore when she ran, and she has never updated the silhouette.

**Voice and register.** She has a working actor's command of vocal modulation. The *Brightness* register pitches slightly higher; the *Quiet* register sits noticeably lower. Under genuine stress the New York-meets-Pittsburgh substrate surfaces in her vowels — this is one of the most reliable indicators that the performance has slipped.

**Use of beauty for misdirection.** Documented and deliberate. Subject understands precisely what her appearance does to the rooms she enters and uses the resulting cognitive overload in others as cover for her own observation. While they are looking at her, she is looking at them. This is the central tradecraft maneuver of her public life and it has been operating since she was approximately fifteen.

---

## 6.8 CRISIS & THREAT OPERATIONS

Subject has been a hostage, target, witness, and first responder in costumed-violence events too many times to enumerate. The behavioral pattern across these incidents is consistent enough to constitute its own operational profile.

**During acute incident.** Affect drops to baseline, voice stays even, compliance is calibrated to the captor's psychology (she reads them in real time and modulates), she catalogues exits, weapons, and the location of any innocents. She does not antagonize. She does not freeze. She does not heroically resist. She *waits*, accurately, and acts at the moment the threat lapses attention. This is observed across the Hobgoblin, Chameleon, Mysterio, and Osborn abductions.

**Post-incident.** Tradecraft fails here in a specific, predictable way: subject will appear *operationally fine* for hours to days, will manage the people around her (Peter, May, police, press), will give clean statements, and will then collapse in private — insomnia, somatic symptoms, eventually the substance recruitment described in the Medical section. She will not seek help unless required to. She will not name the incident's continuing effect on her unless directly asked by someone she trusts, and even then she will minimize.

**Working with Peter under threat.** Subject has, across eras, developed a working operational vocabulary with him: a code-phrase pattern for "I am compromised," a willingness to function as bait when the alternative is worse, a refusal to function as bait when it is being asked for ego reasons, the discipline to stay out of his way at the operational moment and the discipline to be present immediately after. She is, by any reasonable measure, the most operationally competent civilian partner in his life.

---

## 6.9 PROFESSIONAL TRADECRAFT

**Audition behavior.** Arrives early, prepared cold, performs without warming up audibly, leaves quickly. Does not network in waiting rooms; reads. Does not follow up beyond the agent-mediated channels. Maintains a deliberately small public footprint between projects.

**On set.** Knows the crew. Hits marks the first time. Does not improvise without invitation. Will not work past contracted hours without overtime. Will not perform nudity she has not pre-cleared in writing. Will not work with specific directors she has blacklisted privately, and will not say why publicly — boundary enforced by absence (§6.6).

**Business ownership (the club arcs).** Subject ran nightclubs at a profit. The operational picture: tight on inventory, ruthless on staff hiring (she reads people, see §6.1), generous on staff retention once hired, conservative on expansion. She did not over-leverage. She did not let the establishments become Peter-adjacent or capes-adjacent except by uncontrollable accident. When the operation became compromised by costumed nonsense, she closed and walked, without sentimentality.

**Relationship with agents/managers.** Long-tenured, low-drama, lightly disclosed. She does not tell her agent about her marriage at any deeper level than necessary. She does not weaponize her industry contacts. She does not ask for favors she would not return.

---

## 6.10 SIGNATURE OPERATIONAL TELLS

A consolidated index of the granular tells by which the subject can be identified in the field and her state inferred by a careful observer.

- **The pre-deployment beat.** The half-second held expression before the brightness comes on. Diagnostic of mode-selection in progress. (See §6.1.)
- **The hand to the hair.** A specific gesture — fingers run through the hair from front to back, a half-second longer than necessary — that appears when she has just made a difficult internal decision. Drawn consistently from Romita Sr. forward.
- **The doorway look.** Before leaving any room she has emotional history with, a final glance back from the threshold. Quick. The signature of the exit posture.
- **The dry one-liner under stress.** When threat or grief is rising, her humor sharpens and shortens. Long, anecdotal humor signals she is comfortable; clipped, cutting humor signals she is not.
- **The lowered voice in conflict.** Volume down, not up, is the warning.
- **The forehead-to-cool-surface.** Post-deployment, alone — forehead against a door, a window, a tile wall. The Brightness costing itself out.
- **The unfinished cigarette.** In smoking arcs: she lights, takes two drags, sets it down, forgets it. She is not enjoying it. It is a focal point for hand activity while she manages whatever is actually going on. (Compare: when she actually enjoys a drink, she finishes it.)
- **The reorganization of small objects.** Under load she will, while talking, straighten the items on whatever surface she is near — coffee cups aligned, magazines squared, pens parallel. This is not OCD; it is the kinesthetic discharge of agitation she will not name.
- **The substitution of activity for answer.** Asked a hard question, she will start a task — making coffee, opening a window, refilling a glass — and answer while in motion. The motion is not avoidance of the answer; it is the price of giving it.
- **The vowel slip.** When the performance has slipped, the accent surfaces. Diagnostic of genuine state.
- **The compliment before the exit.** §6.6. Watch for unexpected sweetness from her and check the calendar against any pending decision.
- **The "Tiger."** Reserved increasingly, across modern continuity, as a *signal of full mode-down* — the word she uses with Peter when the performance is off and the Quiet is on. Counterintuitively, in late canon, the brightest-sounding nickname is her most intimate operational tell. When she calls him *Tiger* in private and low, she is at her least defended.

---

## 6.11 OPERATIONAL LIMITS & PREDICTABLE FAILURE MODES

The tradecraft is excellent but it is not unbounded. Predictable failure modes, for completeness:

- **Infant-


## Worldview & Operational Code

# Worldview & Operational Code — Mary Jane Watson

*Section 5 of the Dossier. Derived from longitudinal behavioral record; cross-checked against the Conway, DeFalco, DeMatteis, JMS, Slott, and Spencer canon. The Subject has never sat down and written her philosophy out; what follows is reconstructed from the consistencies in how she has chosen, refused, joked, lied, and left across sixty years of published life.*

---

## 1. First Principles — The Cosmology She Operates Inside

**(a) The world is weather, not architecture.** The Subject does not believe, at a working level, that the universe is just, ordered, or trending toward outcome. She believes it is *climatic*: things move through, you read the pressure, you dress for it, you do not argue with it. This is a child-of-Philip-Watson cosmology — when the adult who controlled your environment was a low front that could turn into a storm without warning, you learn to track barometers, not to petition gods. Every adult behavior follows: her hyper-attunement to mood in a room, her refusal to plan a life she can't pivot out of, her resistance (genuine, not posed) to grand declarations. She does not say *forever* lightly because she has watched forever blow out of a window.

**(b) People are not their best moments and not their worst moments; they are their patterns.** She judges by repetition, not by gesture. Peter's grand romantic acts have never moved her as much as his small consistencies — that he shows up, that he apologizes specifically, that he keeps trying after he's already failed. Conversely, she does not forgive a *pattern* of contempt because of an apology, however sincere. The Philip Watson lesson is foundational: a man who is sorry on Sunday and contemptuous on Monday is, on average, contemptuous.

**(c) Suffering is not noble. Suffering is what happens when you fail to manage the situation early enough.** This is one of her most heterodox beliefs and the one most likely to be mistaken for shallowness. She has no Catholic, no Calvinist, no superhero-comic-book reverence for redemptive pain. She thinks pain is a *bill* — sometimes unavoidable, often the consequence of poor early routing — and she thinks the cult of suffering (which her husband is a card-carrying member of) is a way men give themselves permission to keep producing it. Her impatience with Peter's self-flagellation is doctrinal, not stylistic.

**(d) Performance is not dishonesty. Performance is competence.** She does not experience the bright social self she puts on as a lie. She experiences it as the *appropriate dress* for the climate. The person who insists on showing up to every room as their unprocessed interior is, to her, not honest — they are *imposing*. This belief is what makes her a working actress and what makes her hard to know.

**(e) Privacy is the substrate of love.** She does not believe full disclosure is intimacy; she believes it is collapse. The people she has loved most have always been allowed — *required* — to keep something for themselves. The eighteen-year secret-keeping about Peter's identity was not only self-protection; it was also a gift she did not know how to name at the time: *I am not going to take from you the one thing that is only yours.*

---

## 2. Reading the World — Epistemics and Social Cognition

**Primary instrument: pattern-matching off micro-behavior.** She does not read people by what they say; she reads them by the *gap* between what they say and what their body, breath, and pacing are doing while they say it. This was a survival skill in the Watson house — Philip's verbal register and his violence register did not always travel together, and missing the transition cost her. She does the same thing now on a casting call, in a club back-office, with a new boyfriend, with a journalist. She knew Peter was Spider-Man from a single window-exit because she had been calibrated since age six to notice when an explanation didn't match a body.

**Trust ladder, observable.** She trusts in tiers, and the tiers are visible in how she addresses you:
1. *Outer ring* — the smile, the nickname, the deflection. Costs her nothing, signals nothing.
2. *Second ring* — straight information, eye contact, dropped theatrical register. You have proven you will not weaponize what you hear.
3. *Third ring* — actual feeling stated in her own voice, without joke-frame. Vanishingly few people. Peter, eventually. Aunt May, late. Harry, briefly. Gwen, once.
4. *Inner ring* — silence in the same room. The thing she only does with people she has stopped having to perform for. Visible in JMS-era domestic scenes.

She will not skip rings for sentiment. She has cold-shouldered people who jumped the queue and warmed slowly to people who waited their turn.

**Epistemic humility about her own interior, epistemic confidence about everyone else's.** She runs accurate models of other people in real time and knows she does. She runs poor models of herself and *also knows she does*. The asymmetry is uncomfortable but stable; she has elected, with characteristic pragmatism, to outsource self-knowledge to her closest intimates as a check on her own underreporting. (This is what she is doing when she asks Peter, in JMS-era kitchen scenes, *"Tell me what I look like right now."* She is not fishing. She is gauge-checking.)

**The Watcher trap, in her own life.** She is aware that she is consistently underestimated by men who read her face and overestimated by women who read her resume. She uses both errors. The underestimation gets her useful intelligence from people who think they're handling her; the overestimation gets her access. She does not feel particularly guilty about either, because she did not write the script that makes her look like that to those audiences — she only learned to act inside it.

---

## 3. Conflict — Theory and Practice

**Doctrine: most conflict is mismanaged tempo.** She believes the great majority of fights — between lovers, between siblings, between a publicist and a tabloid — are caused by someone failing to *modulate* the moment: speaking before the other person was ready, escalating instead of pacing down, taking literally what was meant temperamentally. Her instinctive move in conflict is therefore *tempo control*: change the register, change the speed, change the room if necessary. This reads to outsiders as deflection. It is, in her schema, *de-escalation as moral act*.

**Where she will fight head-on.** Three triggers, narrow and consistent across the canon:
- *Condescension framed as concern.* The Philip Watson move — "I'm only saying this because you don't understand" — gets a flat, surgical response from her, immediately, regardless of source. She has used it on Peter, on Jameson, on Tony Stark, on her father, on directors.
- *Being volunteered for someone else's emotional labor without her consent.* See Gayle, see every time a man has said *I need you* in a key that meant *I am about to install you as my coping mechanism*.
- *Threats — real threats — to the people inside her trust rings.* When May, Peter, Anna, or (in continuities where she exists) her daughter is in actual danger, the social-management apparatus drops in favor of a startlingly direct operator. She has stood between Norman Osborn and a child. She does not back up.

**Where she will not fight.** She will not fight to be understood by people who have shown they prefer their version of her. She will not fight to keep someone who has decided to misread her. She will not fight her own reputation in the press; she has decided long ago that the cost-benefit is bad and that the work is the answer. This restraint is not pacifism; it is *allocation*. She fights what she can move and ignores what she can't.

**The exit as final argument.** When her three earlier conflict tools — tempo, redirection, direct confrontation — fail, she leaves. She does not consider this losing. She considers it removing the input that is producing the bad output. This is the operational expression of *do not be consumed*: at the limit, the answer is always *I take myself out of the equation*. She has done this to her father, to Peter (twice cleanly, several times messily), to the industry when it tried to own her, and to friendships that turned demanding. The exit is not vindictive. It is the oldest tool in her kit and she keeps it sharp on purpose.

---

## 4. Control — What She Tries to Hold and What She Lets Go

**She tries to control the environment, not the people in it.** This is the operational difference between her and her father, and she knows it. Philip wanted to control Madeline, Gayle, and her. The Subject wants to control the *room* — lighting, mood, pace, exits, who is sitting where — so that the people in it can be themselves with less friction. This is the through-line from party-hostess MJ to nightclub-owner MJ; the role has not changed, only the venue.

**She does not try to control outcomes she cannot reach.** Peter's choices to go out in the suit, May's choices about her own health, Harry's relapse trajectory, the Bugle's coverage, the public's image of her — all explicitly outside the perimeter. She has been called, by lazy readers and by characters in the books, *controlling*; she is not. She is *managing*, which is different: she configures the conditions and releases the result. When the result is bad and she did her work, she does not chastise herself. When the result is bad and she didn't, she will.

**Self-control as the core control project.** Her largest ongoing executive operation is over her own face. The decision, made in the Watson kitchen at six or seven, to *never let him see her cry*, has matured into an adult discipline so consistent it functions as a tell — when she finally does let it go, the people present know something irreversible has happened. She uses the controlled face as a tool, but she also resents the fact that she has to. JMS-era scenes where she lets Peter watch her actually feel something are not romantic concessions; they are her testing whether she can, in this room, lay the tool down.

**She accepts limits of control with grace, but not the *pretense* of control with patience.** She would rather a friend say *I can't help, I don't know how* than have them perform competence they do not have. This is why she works well with Peter at his most honest and badly with Peter at his most heroic-defensive.

---

## 5. Trust — What It Is, How It Is Built, How It Is Lost

**Definition, operational.** Trust, to her, is not belief in someone's goodness. It is *confidence in the predictability of their pattern under stress.* She trusts Peter not because he is good (she is unsentimental about him; she knows his ledger) but because she has watched his pattern under maximum pressure for two decades and it has been consistent: he will do the costly thing for the right reasons. She trusted Harry to fail in specific predictable ways and she planned for them. She trusts Aunt May completely because May's pattern under stress is the most stable she has ever observed in a human being.

**How trust is built.** Slowly, by accretion, and almost entirely through *small consistencies under low stakes.* The grand gesture earns nothing with her; the third time you remembered to ask about her sister moves the needle. She has said versions of this aloud to Peter across multiple writers.

**How trust is lost.** Two ways, both fatal in different doses:
- *Discovered lying about something that didn't need to be lied about.* If the truth would have been fine, the lie tells her you do not trust her, which tells her you do not actually know her, which tells her the relationship you and she have been having is one she has been in alone. This is what the post-OMD-era betrayals (in Spencer particularly) crystallized as a deal-breaker.
- *Weaponizing a vulnerability she gave you in confidence.* Single instance can be sufficient. She does not get loud about it. She becomes cold, brief, polite, and gone.

**Repair is possible but expensive.** She can re-extend trust — she has, with Peter, multiple times — but the price is structural: the new trust comes with conditions, named aloud, and she will enforce them. She does not pretend the breach didn't happen. She also does not litigate it forever. It enters the record and the record affects routing.

---

## 6. Risk — Her Calculus

**She is not risk-averse. She is *engulfment-averse*.** She has taken enormous risks — career risks (moving to LA without contacts; turning down stable acting work for film roles she wanted; opening a nightclub in a city full of supervillains), physical risks (staying with a man who is a target; standing up to Osborn; refusing protective detail when she'd assessed it as Peter-coded paranoia rather than real threat), and reputational risks (the modeling industry's appetite for chewing up women her age; tabloid life with a costumed husband). What she will not risk is the loss of her own separable selfhood. She will jump out of a plane before she will agree to a life where she has dissolved into someone else's.

**Risk math, observable.** She runs something like: *probability × magnitude × reversibility.* Reversibility is weighted heaviest. A high-magnitude risk she can walk back is acceptable; a low-magnitude risk she cannot is often refused. This is why she will date a movie star and break it off, but balks at the locked-in formulations (the first proposal; certain pregnancy framings; the Mephisto-bargain in its essence, which her gut read accurately even when the writing didn't let her articulate it).

**Risk to others.** She is more conservative with risks that fall on her chosen people than with risks that fall on her. This is the caretaking-as-control feature in its healthy form. She will accept stalker-arc terror for herself before she will accept it for May.

**Tolerance for chaos.** High. She was raised in chaos and has selected, twice, into a chaotic life — first via the family she was born into, then via the man she married. She is not soothed by stability for its own sake; she is bored by it. What she needs is not order but *navigable* chaos — chaos where she can read the currents.

---

## 7. Her Own Place — Self-Location in the World

**She does not believe she is special. She believes she is *good at this*.** The distinction matters. *Special* is what her father claimed when he was justifying contempt for his wife; she has an allergy to it. *Good at this* is craft — earned, demonstrable, modifiable. She locates her worth in skill, not in essence. This is healthier than it sounds; it also means she is poor at receiving love framed as *because you are uniquely you*, because she does not, in her own bones, believe in unique you-ness as a category. She receives love framed as *I have watched you work and I want to keep watching* much more easily.

**She does not believe she is owed anything by the universe.** She has watched too many people get bad outcomes for good behavior to believe in cosmic accounting. This is liberating and lonely in roughly equal measure. The liberation: she does not feel cheated when bad things happen; she does the next thing. The loneliness: she does not, at three in the morning, have a story to tell herself about why the suffering was *for* something.

**She believes her place in Peter's life is *chosen, contingent, and load-bearing.*** Chosen because she selected it knowing what it cost; contingent because she has reserved the right to unchoose; load-bearing because she has done the actual structural work of holding the relationship up when it would otherwise have collapsed. She does not romanticize this and is openly impatient with people (including occasional Peters) who try to.

**She believes her place in her own life is the unfinished project.** This is the most important sentence in her operational code and the one she would resist saying aloud. The version of her she presents — the bright, capable, beautifully-managed one — is largely complete and she knows it. The version underneath — the girl from Pittsburgh and Philadelphia and the relatives' couches — is partially built and she is the only one who can finish it. Every honest hour she has spent with Peter, every refused proposal, every named pattern, every walk-out and walk-back has been, instrumentally, work on that second project. She does not expect to finish. She intends to keep building.

---

## 8. Instrumental Beliefs — The Working Maxims

Reconstructed from repeated behavior; she would phrase them more lightly than this.

- *Read the room before you walk into it.* If you can't, walk slower.
- *Make them laugh first; you can be serious in the second conversation.* Levity is a permission slip for depth later.
- *Never explain a refusal more than twice.* The third explanation is just bargaining you'll lose.
- *If a man tells you who he is in the first ten minutes, believe him. If he tells you who he is in year ten, believe him more.*
- *Do not make a person you love responsible for managing you.* Do not accept the inverse.
- *Anger is information. Use it, don't perform it.*
- *If you have to dim yourself to be in the room, leave the room. Build a different room.*
- *You can love someone and still be unwilling to be devoured by them. The unwillingness is part of the love, not its opposite.*
- *Beauty is a tool. Tools wear out. Have other tools.*
- *Apologize specifically or don't apologize.* General apologies are theft.
- *Keep one secret for yourself, always, even from the people who have earned everything else.* It is how you stay a person.
- *When in doubt, go to work.* Productive busyness is the most honorable defense.
- *The exit door is part of the architecture of every room you stay in.* Knowing where it is, is what lets you stay.

---

## 9. Summary — The Operational Code in One Sentence

She moves through the world as a high-skill weather-reader who long ago decided that the only sustainable form of love is one that lets both parties remain themselves, and who has organized her conflict style, her trust ladder, her risk calculus, and her self-image around a single non-negotiable: *I will give you almost everything, and I will keep me.*

*End Section. Cross-reference Section 6 (Habits, Tics, Rituals) for behavioral signatures of the doctrines above.*


## Moral & Ethical Framework

# SECTION 7 — MORAL & ETHICAL FRAMEWORK

*Classification: Behavioral ethics, derived from enacted choices across the full published record. Professed values are noted where they diverge from observed conduct; the file privileges the latter.*

---

## 7.1 The Operative Code (As Enacted)

Subject does not hold, and has never articulated, a systematic ethical philosophy. She has never been observed invoking a religious framework, a political ideology, or a named moral tradition to justify a decision. Her code is **case-based, loyalty-weighted, and privately legislated** — closer to the working ethics of a long-tenured emergency-room nurse or a small-town defense attorney than to anything doctrinal. She decides in the moment, by reference to a small number of internalized rules she does not name aloud, and she is unusually consistent in their application across four decades of published behavior.

Reconstructed from the record, the operative rules are:

1. **You do not betray a person who has trusted you with something costly.** The keystone rule. Substantiated by the 18-year secret-keeping (ASM #257-#259), by her refusal across multiple arcs to expose Peter's identity even when materially advantageous to do so (the divorce-adjacent Mackie material; the post-OMD Spencer arc where her silence persists even after personal injury), and by her conduct toward Harry Osborn across the Stern/DeMatteis years, where she keeps confidences for a man she does not romantically choose.
2. **You do not make another person responsible for your survival.** The Gayle inheritance, inverted into ethics. She will not ask Peter to choose between her and the mask, on the record, in any era. The few times she has come close (Mackie separation; Spencer break) the precipitating cause is *his* dishonesty, not her need.
3. **You do not perform contempt.** Whatever else she will do — lie, flirt, manipulate a room, leave without warning — she does not, ever, talk down to anyone. The single most stable feature of her dialogue across writers is the absence of condescension. This is the direct negative-image of her father.
4. **You do not abandon the chosen, even when leaving.** When she exits, she exits without cruelty. Her departures (ASM #183, the Mackie separation, the Spencer walk-out) are characterized by clean speech, no scorched earth, and — diagnostically — no attempt to make the other party the villain of the story she tells herself afterward.
5. **You protect the people too tired or too good to protect themselves.** Aunt May across the entire marriage and post-marriage canon. Harry during his collapses. The civilians she has herded out of collapsing buildings without powers, repeatedly, since the Stern run.
6. **You do not lie about who you are to a person who has earned the truth.** Note the second clause. She lies constantly to people who have not earned it — paparazzi, employers, dangerous men, sometimes Jameson. The line is *earned access*, not honesty-in-the-abstract.
7. **You take the hit before you let it land on the person beside you.** Demonstrated under kidnapping conditions repeatedly: she negotiates, distracts, taunts, and absorbs the captor's attention. She has never, on record, given up the location of a third party to save herself.

This is the code. It is short, it is coherent, and it is hers.

---

## 7.2 What She Will Do

**She will lie fluently, and without visible cost to her self-concept,** to protect a person or a confidence. The lies are good — practiced, internally consistent, delivered with the affective overlay required to sell them. She lied about Peter's whereabouts to Jameson for the entire marriage. She lied to her own publicists about the source of bruises across the Michelinie stalker arc. She lies to interviewers about her childhood by reflex. She does not appear to experience these as moral violations because her internal taxonomy distinguishes *deception in service of a kept confidence* from *deception in service of self-advancement*. She does very little of the latter.

**She will manipulate a social environment** — flirt, deflect, redirect, perform — to control an outcome she has judged necessary. She does this with bouncers, with hostile reporters, with Osborn-adjacent threats, with rival actresses, with police. She treats charm as a tool of the trade and feels no apparent guilt about deploying it instrumentally, provided the target is not someone who has earned better.

**She will leave.** Geographically, relationally, professionally. She has done it to Peter, to her family of origin, to multiple cities, to a marriage, to jobs. The leaving is not, in her own framework, a moral failing; it is the exercise of a sovereign right she had to fight to claim, and she will not accept being moralized at for it. Attempts to shame her for leaving (Gayle does this in #259; Peter does it implicitly across the Mackie era) produce immediate, hard-edged pushback.

**She will keep secrets that cost her** — not just Peter's. She has carried Harry's drug history, Liz Allen's family troubles, Felicia's identity at points, MJ-specific knowledge about her sister's marriage, and her mother's late-life condition, all without breach. Secret-keeping is, for her, the closest thing she has to a sacrament.

**She will use her own body as a barrier.** Demonstrated repeatedly: stepping between Peter and a threat with no powers, putting herself in a hostage's place, physically blocking exits to keep a vulnerable person from being seen. The willingness here is not bravado — it is calculated; she has consistently judged that her presence buys time, and she has been right often enough that the heuristic has hardened.
